---

# Skill instructions: post-human-normalize

---
name: post-human-normalize
description: Validates and normalizes any file that has been modified by a human reviewer before it becomes input for the next stage. Ensures that human edits have not broken the structural contracts that downstream stages depend on. Runs after every [HUMAN] review checkpoint.
---

# post-human-normalize

## For the human

Every time you review and edit a file — approving factcheck findings, fixing claims, resolving audit issues — there is a risk that your edits break something the next stage depends on. You might merge two claims into one paragraph, accidentally delete a marker, change a citation format, or remove a field from a YAML file. These are easy mistakes to make, and the next AI stage will silently produce wrong output rather than catching the problem.

This step is a quick automated check that catches these problems before they propagate. It runs after every human review point and validates that the edited file still meets the structural requirements for the next stage.

This step does not change the meaning of anything you wrote. It only checks structure, reformats where necessary, and flags violations that need your attention before proceeding.

## When to run

After every `[HUMAN]` checkpoint in the stage order:
- After inventory-audit human review → validates `inventory.approved.yaml`
- After intro-chapter factcheck human review → validates `chapters/00-introduction.adoc`
- After claims-factcheck human review → validates `chapters/NN-<slug>.claims.approved.adoc`
- After narrative-fidelity human review (if REVISE) → validates `chapters/NN-<slug>.adoc`
- After comparative-chapter factcheck human review → validates `comparative.adoc`
- After character-appendix factcheck human review → validates `character-appendix.adoc`

## Checks performed

### For YAML files (`inventory.approved.yaml`, factcheck outputs)
1. All required fields present per the schema defined in the producing skill.
2. No empty required fields.
3. All `source_id` values resolve to entries in `sources.yaml` registry.
4. No YAML syntax errors.

### For claims documents (`*.claims.approved.adoc`)
1. One claim per paragraph — no merged or bundled claims.
2. Every claim paragraph has a footnote citation.
3. Every evidence token (`// EVIDENCE: ...`) is syntactically valid and references a `source_id` in `sources.yaml`.
4. All markers (`[INFERENCE:]`, `[LACUNA:]`, `[RECONSTRUCTION:]`, `[VARIANT:]`) have all required sub-fields.
5. `glossary.yaml` renderings used consistently.

### For prose documents (intro, comparative, character-appendix, narrative chapters)
1. All markers syntactically valid with required sub-fields.
2. All evidence tokens syntactically valid.
3. Canonical chapter anchors present and unchanged.
4. `glossary.yaml` renderings used consistently.
5. No `never_use` glossary terms present.

## Output

The validated file, with a provenance comment block appended:
```
// Validated by post-human-normalize
// Timestamp: <UTC>
// Checks passed: <list>
// Warnings: <any non-blocking issues>
```

If any check fails, the file is returned to the human with a specific error report. The pipeline does not proceed until all checks pass.

## Completion protocol

As your very last action — after all output files are written and all self-checks pass — write a completion record. This allows the pipeline runner to verify that no stage was truncated by a timeout, rate limit, or context overflow.

**File**: `books/<book>/completions/<NN>-<stage-name>.done.yaml`

Create the `completions/` directory if it does not exist.

**Format**:
```yaml
stage: "<stage-name>"
timestamp: "<UTC ISO 8601>"
status: "completed"
agent_model: "<your model name>"
outputs:
  - file: "<relative path from book dir>"
    lines: <line count>
  # repeat for each output file
summary: "<one-line description of what was produced>"
```

**Rules**:
1. Write this file only after ALL outputs are complete and verified.
2. The `lines` count must be the actual line count of each file at the time of writing — do not estimate.
3. If you were unable to complete all outputs, write the file with `status: "partial"` and list which outputs are missing in a `missing` field.
4. Never write `status: "completed"` if any output file is missing or truncated.

## Handoff
The validated file proceeds to the next stage as defined in the stage order.

---

# Input file: chapters/21-gonggong.claims.adoc

```
[[ch-21-gonggong-claims]]
== Gonggong and the Broken Pillar — Claims

// One fact per paragraph; chapter-draft will transform this into narrative.
// Full chapter (CORE tier) on the Chinese cosmogonic combat between Gonggong
// and a sky-emperor (Zhuanxu in the most widely attested Huainanzi version;
// Zhurong in some Shanhaijing-and-commentary variants), the breaking of
// Mount Buzhou, the tilting of the cosmos, and Nüwa's repair of the sky
// with five-coloured stones. Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml, the chapter
// presents the Huainanzi and Lienü zhuan variants side by side under
// co-equal classification (neither is scholarly-privileged), embeds an
// 800-1200-word Yu-the-Great sidebar contrasting the combat-ordering of
// Gonggong with the hydraulic-ordering of Yu, and supplies the chapter's
// cosmogonic credential for Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) by treating
// the post-combat geography (tilted cosmos, rivers flowing east) as the
// principal cosmogonic outcome. Area-specialist citation policy applies:
// Lewis 2006 and Birrell 1993 cited by name in the first factual pass.
// Per brief target_length_words, the chapter targets ~7000 narrative words.

=== Source overview

The chapter treats the Chinese cosmogonic combat in which the water-god Gonggong, defeated in combat with a sky-emperor, strikes the cosmic pillar Mount Buzhou with his head, tilts the cosmos, and triggers the repair-cosmogony in which the serpent-tailed creator-goddess Nüwa patches the sky with five-coloured stones; the combat-and-repair sequence is the single most widely attested early-imperial Chinese cosmogonic-combat narrative.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml; Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the Gonggong / Nüwa broken-pillar cosmogony as the principal early-imperial Chinese cosmogonic-combat narrative; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The Gonggong narrative has no continuous primary-text witness from the Warring States period (5th–3rd c. BCE) in which most scholars would place the formation of the underlying tradition; the surviving narrative is preserved instead in a cluster of Han-dynasty (2nd c. BCE – 2nd c. CE) compilations that draw on earlier oral and written traditions and that supply different parts of the connected story.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the Han-dynasty character of the surviving Gonggong narrative and on the absence of a continuous Warring States primary text; brief 21-gonggong.yaml lacunae entry on the no-complete-Warring-States-source premise.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The principal Han-dynasty witness to the Gonggong / Nüwa cosmogony is the *Huainanzi* (淮南子, "Master of Huainan"), a c. 139 BCE encyclopaedic compilation associated with Liu An, Prince of Huainan, whose chapter 天文訓 ("Treatise on the Patterns of Heaven") preserves the fullest single account of Gonggong's combat with a sky-emperor, his striking of Mount Buzhou, and the resulting tilt of the cosmos.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Huainanzi* 天文訓 chapter as the fullest single account of the Gonggong cosmogony and on its c. 139 BCE Liu-An-of-Huainan compilation context; brief 21-gonggong.yaml lacunae entry on the *Huainanzi* 天文訓 attestation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The *Lienü zhuan* (列女傳, "Categorised Biographies of Women"), conventionally attributed to Liu Xiang (c. 79–8 BCE) and dated to the late Western Han, supplies a parallel Han-period account in which the Gonggong combat is followed by a flood that Yu the Great tames by opening channels — presenting the cosmogony as a sequential drama in which combat-ordering and hydraulic-ordering are linked stages of a single narrative rather than two separate stories.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Lienü zhuan* sequential-drama presentation of the Gonggong cosmogony followed by Yu's flood-taming; on Liu Xiang as conventional compiler and the late Western Han date; brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants entry on the *Lienü zhuan* sequential reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The *Shanhaijing* (山海經, "Classic of Mountains and Seas") supplies a further Han-period (with Warring States stratum elements) witness in which Gonggong is named alongside other primordial figures and attached to multiple, often-incompatible mini-traditions; the *Shanhaijing*'s commentaries (notably the 3rd-century-CE Jin-dynasty commentary of Guo Pu) supply additional Gonggong references that name different opponents (Zhuanxu, Zhurong, and others) for the combat.footnote:[Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the *Shanhaijing* as a fragmentary witness to the Gonggong material and on the multiple-opponents pattern across the *Shanhaijing* and its commentaries; brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants entry on the *Shanhaijing*-and-commentaries multiple-opponents pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=chapters on cosmogony

The *Liezi* (列子, "Master Lie"), a heterogeneous Daoist compilation in its received form conventionally dated to the 3rd–4th century CE but drawing on earlier material, supplies a further later witness to the Gonggong / Nüwa cosmogony with characteristic Daoist-philosophical framing of the combat-and-repair sequence as a cosmological aetiology.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Liezi* as a later-but-load-bearing compilation witness to the Gonggong / Nüwa cosmogony; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the *Liezi*'s Daoist-philosophical framing of the cosmogonic material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter accordingly treats the Gonggong narrative as a Han-period composite reconstructed from these compilation-witnesses (*Huainanzi*, *Lienü zhuan*, *Shanhaijing* with commentaries, *Liezi*) rather than as a single connected primary text, and the chapter's variant-handling follows the brief's co-equal classification: the *Huainanzi* and the *Lienü zhuan* versions are presented side by side without privileging either, and the *Shanhaijing*-and-commentary variants are presented as legitimate parallel mini-traditions rather than as deviations from a postulated original.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the co-equal variant classification across the Han-period compilations and on the side-by-side presentation of *Huainanzi* and *Lienü zhuan*; Lewis 2006, chs. 2–3, on the composite-reconstruction character of the surviving Gonggong narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's principal English-language scholarly anchor is Mark Edward Lewis's *The Flood Myths of Early China*, published by SUNY Press in 2006, whose chapters 2–3 supply the standard English-language synthesis of the Gonggong cycle (the broken-pillar cosmogony, Nüwa's repair, the Yu-the-Great flood-taming sequel) and whose other chapters on Yu supply the apparatus for the present chapter's embedded Yu sidebar.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China* (Albany: SUNY Press, 2006; ISBN 9780791466636), chs. 2–3 on the Gonggong / Nüwa broken-pillar cosmogony as the chapter's principal anchor and chapters on Yu used for the embedded sidebar; brief 21-gonggong.yaml sources.primary entry on Lewis 2006 as the chapter's principal anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

Lewis 2006 is printed-only in the present project's working corpus, and the chapter cites it at chapter level (chs. 2–3 for the Gonggong / Nüwa material; Yu chapters for the sidebar) per the citation discipline applied to the East Asian cluster of the present book.footnote:[Lewis 2006, chs. 2–3 (printed-only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline of the present book's East Asian cluster); brief 21-gonggong.yaml on chapter-level citation discipline.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's secondary anchor is Anne Birrell's *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, published by Johns Hopkins University Press in 1993, whose chapters on cosmogony supply a complementary general-survey treatment of the Gonggong narrative including the *Shanhaijing*-and-commentary variants and the multiple-opponents pattern across the corpus.footnote:[Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction* (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 1993; ISBN 9780801849954), chapters on cosmogony as the chapter's secondary anchor for the Gonggong / Nüwa material and the *Shanhaijing*-and-commentary variants; brief 21-gonggong.yaml sources.primary entry on Birrell 1993.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=chapters on cosmogony

Birrell 1993 is also printed-only and is cited at chapter level in the present chapter, with the chapters-on-cosmogony locus the load-bearing reference for general-survey claims about the Gonggong corpus.footnote:[Birrell 1993, chapters on cosmogony (printed-only; cited at chapter level per the established discipline); brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the secondary-anchor role of Birrell 1993.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=chapters on cosmogony

The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth tradition is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton University Press, 1987), whose treatment of non-Near-Eastern combat-myth supplies a wider scholarly anchor for the Gonggong narrative's place in the comparative dragon-combat corpus and for the chapter's forward-link to Part II C1 of the present book.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, treatment of non-Near-Eastern combat-myth as background to the Gonggong narrative and to the present chapter's forward-link to Part II C1; brief 21-gonggong.yaml sources.secondary entry on Forsyth 1987.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on non-Near-Eastern combat-myth

The chapter is a CORE-tier full-chapter treatment, organising the Gonggong / Nüwa cosmogony as a connected narrative across the Han-period compilation-witnesses, with the Yu-the-Great material treated as an embedded 800–1200-word sidebar within the chapter rather than expanded into a separate chapter; per brief target_length_words the chapter aims at approximately 7,000 narrative words including the sidebar.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the CORE-tier classification, the embedded Yu sidebar at 800–1200 words within the chapter, and the 7,000-word target for the chapter as a whole; scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 9 on the Yu-the-Great sidebar within the Gonggong chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's framing premise is that the Gonggong narrative supplies one of the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases across non-cognate traditions for the present book's Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony), alongside Marduk-Tiamat in the *Enūma Eliš*, Ninurta-Asag in *Lugal-e*, and Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec — the post-combat geography of the tilted cosmos and the east-flowing rivers being the chapter's load-bearing cosmogonic credential.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml comparative_hooks on Part II C1's pairing of Gonggong with Marduk, Ninurta/Asag, and Huitzilopochtli as the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases; Lewis 2006, chs. 2–3, on the post-combat geography as the chapter's cosmogonic credential.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

=== Setting and combatants

The Gonggong of the *Huainanzi*-and-related Han-period sources is a primordial water-god, conventionally described in the surviving accounts and later commentaries as red-haired and serpent-bodied (or human-faced with serpent body), and is positioned within the early-imperial Chinese pantheon as one of the pre-human emperor-figures whose combats with one another are part of the succession of cosmogonic episodes that establishes the present cosmos.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Gonggong as a primordial water-god of the early-imperial Chinese pantheon, with red-haired and serpent-bodied descriptions in the surviving Han and post-Han sources; brief 21-gonggong.yaml entities_glossary entry on Gonggong as water-god, often red-haired and serpent-bodied; glossary 'Gonggong' first_mention_gloss on the same description.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

Gonggong's role as water-god supplies one of the load-bearing motifs of the cosmogonic-combat narrative: his combats are connected in the Han-period sources with floods, with the obstruction or release of waters, and with the channelling of rivers, and the post-combat hydrography of the cosmos (the eastward flow of the Chinese rivers to the sea) is one of the chief aetiological outcomes of the broken-pillar episode.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Gonggong's role as water-god in the cosmogonic combat and on the post-combat hydrography of the eastward-flowing rivers; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the same hydrographic-aetiology pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

Zhuanxu (顓頊), the sky-emperor who defeats Gonggong in the most widely attested *Huainanzi* version of the combat, is one of the so-called Five Emperors of the early-imperial Chinese tradition — a sequence of pre-human or semi-divine sovereign figures whose successive reigns supply the framework within which the cosmogonic episodes are organised — and his defeat of Gonggong is read in the Han sources as part of the establishment of his legitimate sovereignty over the cosmos.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Zhuanxu as the sky-emperor of the most widely attested *Huainanzi* version of the combat and on his place within the Five-Emperors sequence; brief 21-gonggong.yaml entities_glossary entry on Zhuanxu as "the sky-emperor who defeats Gonggong in the most widely attested version"; glossary 'Zhuanxu' first_mention_gloss on the same role.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

Zhurong (祝融), a fire-god, appears in some *Shanhaijing* and commentary variants as the alternative combatant who defeats Gonggong, with the substitution of fire-god for sky-emperor reflecting the multiple-mini-traditions character of the Gonggong corpus rather than a single privileged opponent across the surviving sources.footnote:[Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on Zhurong as fire-god alternative combatant in *Shanhaijing* and commentary variants; brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants entry on the *Shanhaijing*-and-commentaries multiple-opponents pattern; glossary 'Zhurong' first_mention_gloss on the fire-god / alternative-combatant role.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=chapters on cosmogony

[VARIANT: primary=*Huainanzi* 天文訓 c. 139 BCE: Gonggong fights Zhuanxu (the sky-emperor) and loses; in rage, he strikes Mount Buzhou with his head, breaking the sky-pillar and tilting the cosmos | alt=*Shanhaijing* and its commentaries (e.g., Guo Pu's 3rd-c. CE commentary): Gonggong fights different opponents (Zhuanxu in some variants, Zhurong in others, and further figures in still others), with the combat-narrative fragmented into multiple mini-traditions | chosen: present-side-by-side | reason: per brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants.classification = co-equal; the *Huainanzi* version is the fullest single account but is itself a 2nd c. BCE compilation rather than a continuous Warring States primary text, and Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 and Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony both treat the multiple-opponents pattern as a legitimate feature of the Gonggong corpus rather than a deviation; the chapter's first factual pass cites Zhuanxu as the most widely attested opponent while registering Zhurong and others as alternative attestations.]

Nüwa (女媧), the serpent-tailed creator-goddess who repairs the broken sky after the combat, is one of the principal cosmogonic figures of the early-imperial Chinese pantheon and is associated more broadly with the creation of humans (in some Han sources, by moulding humans from yellow earth) and with the mending and ordering of the cosmos; her role in the Gonggong narrative is the post-combat repair-cosmogony rather than the combat itself.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Nüwa as serpent-tailed creator-goddess and post-combat repairer of the sky in the Gonggong cycle; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on Nüwa's wider creation-and-mending role across the Han sources; brief 21-gonggong.yaml entities_glossary entry on Nüwa as serpent-tailed creator-goddess; glossary 'Nüwa' first_mention_gloss on the same.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

Mount Buzhou (不周山), the cosmic pillar broken by Gonggong's head-blow, has a name conventionally rendered "mountain-that-is-not-whole" or "incomplete mountain", and is positioned in the Han-period cosmography as one of the pillars supporting the sky in the northwest quadrant of the cosmos; the name itself is read by Lewis 2006 as etymologically connected to the post-combat tilted-cosmos outcome — the mountain is "not-whole" because it is broken by the combat.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Mount Buzhou's name "mountain-that-is-not-whole" and on its position as a sky-pillar in the northwest of the Han-period cosmography; brief 21-gonggong.yaml entities_glossary entry on Mount Buzhou; glossary 'Mount Buzhou' first_mention_gloss on "mountain-that-is-not-whole" rendering.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The Han-period cosmography that the *Huainanzi* presupposes positions the cosmos as a roughly square earth covered by a roughly circular heaven, with sky-supporting pillars at the cardinal-and-intercardinal points; Mount Buzhou is one such pillar, and its breaking is therefore a structurally-cosmological event with consequences for the whole geometry of the cosmos rather than a merely local catastrophe.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the Han-period cosmography of square-earth-and-circular-heaven with sky-pillars and on Mount Buzhou's structurally-cosmological role; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the same cosmographic framework.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

=== The combat and the broken pillar (Huainanzi version)

In the *Huainanzi* 天文訓 version, the combat between Gonggong and Zhuanxu is precipitated by a contest for sovereignty: Gonggong, defeated in the contest, reacts with rage and turns his violence on the cosmic pillar itself rather than on his victorious opponent — a displacement of the combat from a duel between persons to an attack on the cosmographic structure that the Han text presents as the mechanism by which the cosmogonic outcome is produced.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Huainanzi* 天文訓 sovereignty-contest framing of the Gonggong-Zhuanxu combat and on the displacement of Gonggong's rage from his opponent to the cosmic pillar; brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the Huainanzi version as "Gonggong fights Zhuanxu and loses; in rage, he strikes Mount Buzhou".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

Gonggong, in his rage at the defeat, strikes Mount Buzhou with his head: the *Huainanzi* presents the head-blow as the specific physical mechanism by which the sky-pillar is broken, with Gonggong's serpent-bodied form supplying the impulse and the head supplying the destructive contact, and the resulting fracture of the pillar is the load-bearing event of the broken-pillar cosmogony.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Huainanzi*'s head-blow mechanism for the breaking of Mount Buzhou and on the load-bearing role of the head-blow in the broken-pillar cosmogony; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the same head-blow detail in the surviving Han sources.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The breaking of Mount Buzhou produces a structural tilt in the cosmos: the *Huainanzi* records that with the northwestern sky-pillar broken, the heavens tilt down toward the northwest and the earth correspondingly tilts down toward the southeast, and the tilt is the principal aetiological outcome of the combat — it is what the Han-period reader is meant to learn the broken-pillar story in order to understand.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Huainanzi*'s northwest-heaven-tilt and southeast-earth-tilt and on the tilt as the principal aetiological outcome of the Gonggong combat; brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the cosmic tilt as the load-bearing cosmogonic outcome.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The cosmic tilt explains, on the *Huainanzi*'s reading, the empirical asymmetry of the Han-period observed cosmos: the heavenly bodies (sun, moon, stars) appear to move toward the northwest because heaven is tilted toward the northwest, and the rivers of the Chinese landscape flow eastward to the sea because the earth is tilted toward the southeast — the broken-pillar combat is the aetiology of the present geography of the world.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Huainanzi*'s aetiological reading of the cosmic tilt as the explanation for the apparent northwest-motion of heavenly bodies and the east-flowing rivers; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the same hydrographic and astronomical aetiology pattern; brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the post-combat geography (tilted cosmos, rivers flowing east) as the cosmogonic credential for Part II C1.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The eastward flow of the rivers is one of the load-bearing cosmogonic outcomes that the chapter presents as the Gonggong narrative's principal contribution to the present book's Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) argument: the Han text explicitly connects an empirical-and-everyday feature of Chinese geography (the rivers flowing east to the sea) to the post-combat tilt of the earth, and the connection makes the broken-pillar combat a cosmogony in the strong sense — the present cosmos is what the combat produced.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the eastward-rivers aetiology as a cosmogony in the strong sense; brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.cross_cultural_parallels on Gonggong as cosmogonic-combat parallel to Marduk in the present book's Part II C1.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

=== Nüwa and the repair of the sky

After the breaking of the sky-pillar and the resulting tilt of the cosmos, the *Huainanzi* and parallel Han sources record that Nüwa undertakes the repair of the broken sky: she melts five-coloured stones (五色石) and uses the molten material to patch the gap in the heavens — the smelting-and-patching is the central technical operation of the repair-cosmogony.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Nüwa's smelting of five-coloured stones to patch the broken sky as the central technical operation of the repair-cosmogony; brief 21-gonggong.yaml entities_glossary entry on five-coloured stones (五色石) as the artefact Nüwa melts.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The five-coloured stones are presented in the Han sources as a technical-cosmological material rather than as a generic patch: their five colours are read in subsequent Han and post-Han Chinese cosmological writing as corresponding to the Five Phases or Five Elements (wuxing 五行) — wood, fire, earth, metal, water — that organise the cosmographic system within which the repair makes sense.footnote:[Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the five-coloured stones' connection with the Five Phases / wuxing system in subsequent Han and post-Han cosmological writing; Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3, on the technical-cosmological character of the five-coloured-stones material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=chapters on cosmogony

In the *Huainanzi*'s account, Nüwa supplements the smelted-stone patch with a further structural intervention: she cuts the legs from a giant turtle (in some versions, a giant tortoise) and uses them to prop up the corners of the heaven, restoring a measure of structural support to replace the broken Mount Buzhou and to stabilise the tilted cosmos against collapse.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Nüwa's use of the legs of a giant turtle to prop up the corners of the heaven after the breaking of Mount Buzhou; brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants entry on Nüwa's repair "with five-coloured stones and the legs of a giant turtle" in the *Huainanzi* version.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The turtle-leg intervention is presented in the *Huainanzi* as a partial rather than a full repair: the broken Mount Buzhou is not restored to its original integrity, the cosmic tilt is not undone, and the post-combat cosmos retains the asymmetry that the combat produced — Nüwa's repair stabilises the cosmos in its tilted state rather than returning it to a pre-combat condition.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on Nüwa's repair as a partial-rather-than-full intervention that stabilises the tilted cosmos in its post-combat state rather than restoring the pre-combat integrity; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on the partial-repair character of the Han sources' treatment.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The combined combat-and-repair sequence — Gonggong breaks the sky-pillar; Nüwa stabilises the tilted cosmos with smelted stones and turtle-legs — is therefore presented in the Han sources as a single connected cosmogony in which the present asymmetric cosmos emerges through a two-stage process: a destructive combat that generates the tilt, and a constructive repair that fixes the tilt in its present form.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the combat-and-repair sequence as a single connected cosmogony in two stages (destructive combat + constructive repair); brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the combat-plus-repair as the explanation for the present asymmetric cosmos.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the combat-and-repair sequence as a single connected cosmogony in which Gonggong's combat and Nüwa's repair are linked stages of one narrative — rather than as two structurally separable episodes — is a synthesis drawn from Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 and Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony, both of which present the *Huainanzi* and parallel Han accounts as containing both elements but neither of which provides a single quoted phrase joining them in the strong "two stages of one cosmogony" formulation that the chapter adopts. | basis: Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 treats the combat and the repair as parts of one *Huainanzi* narrative-stretch, and the parallel passages in Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony do likewise; the brief's cultural_relevance.function entry presents "the combat-plus-repair" as the joint explanation for the cosmic asymmetry. | risk: alternative readings within the comparative literature on Chinese cosmogony are possible — some scholars treat the Nüwa-repair as a structurally separable cosmogonic episode whose connection to the Gonggong combat is itself a Han-period editorial joining of older independent traditions; the chapter's two-stages-of-one-cosmogony framing is the editorial choice favoured by the brief's combat-plus-repair phrasing and by Lewis 2006's connected-narrative presentation, but it is a reading rather than an attested feature of the Han sources.]

=== The Lienü zhuan and the flood (co-equal Han variant)

The *Lienü zhuan* version of the Gonggong narrative connects the broken-pillar combat to a subsequent flood: the combat damages the cosmos in such a way that water either obstructs or escapes its proper channels, and the resulting flood becomes the disorder that Yu the Great is then summoned to remedy through hydraulic means.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Lienü zhuan* connection of the Gonggong combat to a subsequent flood that Yu remedies hydraulically; brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants entry on "Lienü zhuan and other Han sources: Gonggong's combat is followed by the flood, which Yu the Great tames by opening channels".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

In the *Lienü zhuan*'s sequential reading, the Gonggong combat and the Yu flood-taming are not two separate stories but stages of a single connected drama: the combat breaks the cosmographic structure that ordinarily contains the waters; the broken structure releases a flood; Yu then tames the flood by opening channels and re-channelling the rivers; and the post-Yu hydrography is the present-day flow of the Chinese rivers to the eastern sea.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the *Lienü zhuan*'s sequential drama of Gonggong-combat → flood → Yu-taming → present-day hydrography; brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants entry on the sequential cosmogonic-drama character of the *Lienü zhuan* presentation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The *Lienü zhuan* sequential-drama presentation is therefore not a competing account of the Gonggong combat but a parallel Han-period framing in which the cosmogonic combat is one stage of a longer ordering-process that includes both the combat-disruption and the hydraulic-restoration; the chapter's co-equal variant-handling presents this framing alongside the *Huainanzi*'s combat-and-Nüwa-repair framing rather than choosing between them.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the parallel-rather-than-competing relationship of the *Huainanzi* combat-and-Nüwa-repair version and the *Lienü zhuan* combat-and-Yu-taming version; per brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants.classification = co-equal on side-by-side presentation of the two Han variants without scholarly privileging.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The two co-equal Han variants share their cosmogonic outcome (the present-day asymmetric cosmos with east-flowing rivers) but differ in their distribution of the ordering-work between divine repair (Nüwa's smelted stones and turtle-legs in the *Huainanzi*) and human-or-semi-divine technical labour (Yu's channel-opening in the *Lienü zhuan*); the chapter treats the difference as a substantive feature of the Han Gonggong corpus rather than as a contradiction to be resolved.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the substantive difference between the divine-repair and human-technical-labour distributions of the ordering-work across the *Huainanzi* and *Lienü zhuan* variants; per brief 21-gonggong.yaml variants entry on neither variant being scholarly-privileged.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

=== Yu the Great — embedded sidebar

Yu the Great (大禹) is, in the broader early-imperial Chinese tradition, the post-combat flood-controller whose hydraulic ordering of the rivers is one of the foundational episodes of the early-Chinese cosmographic-and-political narrative; the present chapter embeds an 800–1200-word sidebar on Yu within the Gonggong chapter to show that the same Chinese tradition produces two different templates for cosmic ordering — combat (Gonggong) and channel-opening (Yu) — that operate side by side rather than in competition.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chapters on Yu used for the embedded sidebar, on Yu's role as post-combat flood-controller and on the two-templates pattern (combat-ordering and channel-opening) within the same Chinese tradition; brief 21-gonggong.yaml special_instructions on the embedded Yu sidebar within the chapter; scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 9 on the same.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Yu chapters

Yu's defining technical operation is channel-opening: faced with the floods that follow the cosmographic disruption (whether the Gonggong combat in the *Lienü zhuan* sequential reading or other flood-narratives in the wider tradition), Yu does not contend with a personalised flood-adversary in combat but rather opens channels and re-routes the waters — turning hydraulic engineering rather than dragon-slaying into the cosmographic-restoration mechanism.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chapters on Yu, on Yu's channel-opening as the defining technical operation of his flood-control and on the channel-opening / dragon-slaying contrast as a structural feature of the Chinese flood-myth corpus; brief 21-gonggong.yaml special_instructions on the channel-opening / combat contrast as the sidebar's principal contribution.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Yu chapters

The Yu narrative is closely associated in the Han and post-Han Chinese tradition with the founding of the Xia dynasty — the first dynasty in the conventional Chinese historiographical tradition — and Yu's flood-control work is presented as the direct basis for his receipt of the imperial mandate from Shun (the previous sage-emperor); the hydraulic-ordering is the legitimating act of the founding sovereign.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chapters on Yu, on Yu's flood-control as the legitimating basis for his receipt of the imperial mandate from Shun and on the founding of the Xia dynasty; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, chapters on cosmogony, on Yu's place within the Five-Emperors-and-Three-Dynasties succession.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Yu chapters

The Yu / Gonggong contrast within the same Chinese tradition is therefore a contrast between two different templates for the work of cosmic ordering: the Gonggong narrative produces the present cosmos by combat-and-repair (a destructive combat between divine combatants generates the cosmographic asymmetry; a divine repair stabilises the asymmetry in its present form); the Yu narrative produces present-day order by hydraulic engineering (a sage-king opens channels to direct the waters; the ordering is a technical-administrative act rather than a combat).footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 and Yu chapters, on the combat-template / hydraulic-template contrast within the same Chinese tradition; brief 21-gonggong.yaml special_instructions on the two-templates-coexisting reading as the sidebar's load-bearing observation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3; Yu chapters

The two templates do not contradict each other within the Chinese tradition: the *Lienü zhuan* sequential reading explicitly chains them (Gonggong combat → flood → Yu hydraulic restoration), and the wider Han-and-post-Han Chinese cosmological writing accommodates both as legitimate accounts of cosmic ordering; the contrast is therefore a contrast within a single tradition rather than between different traditions, and that intra-traditional contrast is the sidebar's principal contribution to the present book's wider argument.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 and Yu chapters, on the chained relationship of the two templates in the *Lienü zhuan* and on the wider Han-and-post-Han accommodation of both as legitimate accounts; per brief 21-gonggong.yaml special_instructions on the intra-traditional contrast as the sidebar's principal contribution.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3; Yu chapters

The Yu sidebar's relationship to scope.md's wider classification is that Yu is a PROVISIONAL / methodological case rather than a CORE chaoskampf: per scope.md §"In-scope catalog", Yu is held as the dedicated sidebar that contrasts hydraulic-ordering with combat-ordering, and is explicitly not classified as a chaoskampf within the present book — the sidebar's job is to mark this boundary rather than to expand into a Yu-as-combat-myth treatment.footnote:[Per scope.md §"In-scope catalog" on Yu the Great as a PROVISIONAL / Methodological case treated as a sidebar rather than as a chaoskampf chapter; per brief 21-gonggong.yaml special_instructions on the same boundary-marking role for the Yu sidebar within the Gonggong chapter; Lewis 2006, Yu chapters, on Yu's hydraulic-rather-than-combat role.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Yu chapters

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the Gonggong / Yu pairing as "two different templates for cosmic order coexisting within the same tradition" is the chapter's editorial framing of the Lewis 2006 material on the two figures, and is the load-bearing observation for the present book's argument that combat is one template among several for cosmic ordering rather than a universal or privileged one; the framing draws on Lewis 2006's connected presentation of the Gonggong and Yu materials but is the chapter's own articulation of the two-templates point. | basis: Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 on Gonggong; Lewis 2006 Yu chapters on Yu; *Lienü zhuan*'s sequential chaining of the two; brief 21-gonggong.yaml special_instructions on the two-templates observation as the sidebar's load-bearing point. | risk: the two-templates framing is a comparative-typological reading rather than a direct quotation from the Han sources or from Lewis 2006; alternative framings within Chinese-mythology scholarship would emphasise the chronological priority of the Gonggong combat over the Yu flood-control or read both as moments within a single longer ordering-process rather than as parallel templates.]

=== Cultural and ritual context

The Gonggong cosmogony is preserved across multiple Han-era compilations (*Huainanzi*, *Lienü zhuan*, commentaries on the *Shanhaijing*, *Liezi*) and is the single most widely attested early-imperial Chinese cosmogonic-combat narrative — the attestation-volume across the Han corpus is itself a measure of the narrative's centrality within the early-imperial Chinese cosmological imagination.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the multi-compilation attestation of the Gonggong cosmogony across *Huainanzi*, *Lienü zhuan*, *Shanhaijing*-commentaries, and *Liezi* and on its position as the most widely attested early-imperial Chinese cosmogonic-combat narrative; brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.centrality_evidence on the same multi-attestation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The cosmogony is less directly liturgical than its Near Eastern counterparts (the *Enūma Eliš* recited at the akītu festival; the Hittite Illuyanka myth recited at the purulli) but is nonetheless cited in Han ritual and political texts to explain the asymmetric tilt of the cosmos and the empirical features of Chinese geography (notably why rivers flow east), supplying a cosmological-aetiological function that is invoked in argumentative-rather-than-ritual contexts within Han literary culture.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the less-directly-liturgical character of the Gonggong cosmogony relative to Near Eastern cyclical-maintenance combat-myths and on its citation in Han ritual and political texts to explain the cosmic tilt; brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.centrality_evidence on the ritual-context observation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The cosmogony's royal-legitimation function is explicit in the Han sources: the sky-emperors (Zhuanxu in the *Huainanzi*; Zhurong in the *Shanhaijing*-commentary variants) defeat Gonggong as legitimate rulers, and the combat is presented as part of the succession of pre-human emperors whose victories establish the cosmographic-and-political order within which the human dynasties (Xia, Shang, Zhou) subsequently arise.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the royal-legitimation function of the Gonggong combat within the succession of pre-human emperors and on the connection between the combat and the establishment of the cosmographic-and-political order; brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.function entry on royal-legitimation as a function of the combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The Gonggong cosmogony is treated in the comparative-mythology literature as the standard Chinese cosmogonic-combat comparandum: Lewis 2006 in particular presents the narrative as the principal Chinese case in the comparative flood-and-cosmogony literature, and the chapter's place within the present book's Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) is in line with that scholarly placement.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the Gonggong narrative as the standard Chinese cosmogonic-combat comparandum in the comparative flood / cosmogony literature; brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.centrality_evidence on the same scholarly-placement observation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The Gonggong narrative's position within the present book's subtype-typology is firmly cosmogonic in the strong sense: the present features of the cosmos (heaven's northwestern tilt, earth's southeastern tilt, the eastward flow of the rivers, the empirical asymmetries of Chinese geography) are the direct outcome of the combat, and the combat is therefore a once-only-cosmogonic event rather than a cyclically-maintained one or an eschatologically-displaced one.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the strongly-cosmogonic, once-only character of the Gonggong combat; scope.md §"Subject definition" on the four subtypes (cosmogonic, sovereignty, cyclical-maintenance, eschatological) within which the chapter's classification operates; brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the cosmogonic credential for Part II C1.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's principal cross-cultural comparative companions, on Lewis 2006's framing and on the present book's Part II C1 organisation, are the Mesopotamian Marduk-Tiamat combat (chapter 3 of the present volume), the Sumerian Ninurta-Asag combat (chapter 1), and the Mesoamerican Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec combat (chapter 24 of the present volume): each is a combat-cosmogony in the strong sense in which the present features of the cosmos emerge from the combat's aftermath.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 on the comparative-mythology placement of the Gonggong narrative alongside Marduk-Tiamat, Ninurta-Asag, and Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec; brief 21-gonggong.yaml comparative_hooks on Part II C1's pairing of Gonggong with Marduk, Ninurta/Asag, and Huitzilopochtli.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth tradition is Forsyth 1987's treatment of non-Near-Eastern combat-myth, which supplies a wider scholarly anchor for the Gonggong narrative's place in the comparative dragon-combat corpus and for the framing of the cosmogonic-combat type as a recurring structural feature across non-cognate traditions.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, treatment of non-Near-Eastern combat-myth as background to the Gonggong narrative's place within the comparative dragon-combat corpus; brief 21-gonggong.yaml sources.secondary entry on Forsyth 1987.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on non-Near-Eastern combat-myth

The Gonggong narrative is treated only marginally in Scurlock and Beal 2013's reassessment of Gunkel's chaoskampf hypothesis: the volume's reconsideration of the chaoskampf category focuses on Mesopotamian and Near-Eastern material, and limited references to Chinese cosmogonic-combat material appear in the volume's wider survey of the cross-cultural reception of the chaoskampf framing rather than as load-bearing case studies.footnote:[Scurlock and Beal 2013, *Creation and Chaos: A Reconsideration of Hermann Gunkel's Chaoskampf Hypothesis*, limited references to Chinese cosmogonic-combat material in the volume's wider survey rather than as load-bearing case studies; brief 21-gonggong.yaml sources.secondary entry on Scurlock & Beal 2013.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=scurlock-beal-2013 ; loc=limited references to Chinese material

[LACUNA: a complete connected Warring States primary text for the Gonggong combat-and-repair narrative is not preserved in the surviving corpus | source: per brief 21-gonggong.yaml lacunae entry; Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 on the Han-period composite character of the surviving narrative | scholarly_reconstruction: the narrative is reconstructed from the Han-period compilation-witnesses (*Huainanzi* 天文訓 c. 139 BCE; *Lienü zhuan* late Western Han; *Shanhaijing* with Guo Pu's 3rd-c. CE commentary; *Liezi* in its received 3rd–4th c. CE form), with no single Han or pre-Han text preserving the connected combat-and-repair sequence as a continuous narrative; the chapter accordingly treats the narrative as a Han-period composite reconstruction rather than as a single primary-text witness, and the variant-handling across the *Huainanzi* and *Lienü zhuan* (and the *Shanhaijing*-and-commentary multiple-opponents pattern) reflects the composite character of the surviving evidence per Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 and Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony.]

=== Forward and back links

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony), in which the Gonggong narrative is paired with the Mesopotamian Marduk-Tiamat (*Enūma Eliš*) and Sumerian Ninurta-Asag (*Lugal-e*) combats and the Mesoamerican Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec combat as the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases across non-cognate traditions; the chapter accordingly feeds C1 as a CORE-tier exemplar of the cosmogonic subtype rather than as a borderline or disputed case.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml comparative_hooks on the Part II C1 pairing of Gonggong with Marduk, Ninurta/Asag, and Huitzilopochtli; Lewis 2006, chs. 2–3, on the Gonggong narrative's strongly-cosmogonic character; scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C1 on combat as cosmogony.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's secondary forward-link is to the wider East Asian cluster of the present book: chapter 22 (the family chapter on other Chinese combats, including Hou Yi's archery against the ten suns) follows the present chapter directly in the East Asian cluster, and chapter 23 (Susanoo and Yamata no Orochi, with the chaoskampf framing applied from outside per Naumann / Isomae / Como) follows the family chapter; the present chapter's broken-pillar cosmogony supplies the East Asian cluster's principal cosmogonic-combat reference.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 9 on the East Asian cluster's ordering (Gonggong full chapter; family chapter on other Chinese combats including Hou Yi; Susanoo / Orochi full chapter; Yu sidebar embedded within Gonggong); brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the chapter's position within cluster 9.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's principal back-links within the present book are to the Mesopotamian cosmogonic-combat chapters (chapter 1 Ninurta-Asag *Lugal-e* and chapter 3 Marduk-Tiamat *Enūma Eliš*) and to the Mesoamerican Huitzilopochtli chapter (chapter 24): the Gonggong narrative is the third (with Huitzilopochtli the fourth) of the four Part II C1 exemplars, and the chapter's framing of the broken-pillar combat as a strongly-cosmogonic event explicitly invokes the Marduk and Ninurta companions as the comparative anchors.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml comparative_hooks on the back-links to Marduk-Tiamat, Ninurta-Asag, and Huitzilopochtli; Lewis 2006, chs. 2–3, on the comparative-mythology framing of the Gonggong narrative; scope.md §"Book structure" Part I clusters 1, 9, and 10 on the relative positions of the Mesopotamian, Chinese, and Mesoamerican cosmogonic-combat chapters.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's hydrography back-link is to chapter 1 (Ninurta-Asag): Gonggong's water-releasing-through-broken-earth and the post-combat eastward flow of the Chinese rivers parallels Ninurta's post-combat reordering of the Tigris in *Lugal-e*, with both narratives presenting a storm-or-water-god shaping the present-day hydrography of the cosmos through the combat's outcome; the parallel is structural rather than genetic, since the two traditions are non-cognate.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.cross_cultural_parallels on the Gonggong / Ninurta hydrography-shaping parallel; Lewis 2006, chs. 2–3, on the Gonggong-hydrography aetiology; van Dijk 1983 on the Ninurta-Tigris reordering in *Lugal-e* as the Mesopotamian companion-case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter's geographical-aetiology back-link is to chapter 24 (Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec): both the Gonggong combat (with Mount Buzhou as the broken cosmic pillar) and the Huitzilopochtli combat (with Coatepec — "Mountain of Serpents" — as the cosmic mountain re-embodied as the Templo Mayor) are combat-cosmogonies with explicit geographical aetiology built into the narrative, with the post-combat geography (the broken-pillar tilt; the Templo-Mayor ritual landscape) supplying the load-bearing cosmographic outcome of each combat.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml cultural_relevance.cross_cultural_parallels on the Gonggong / Huitzilopochtli geographical-aetiology parallel; Lewis 2006, chs. 2–3, on Mount Buzhou as the broken cosmic pillar; Matos Moctezuma 1988 on Coatepec as the cosmic mountain re-embodied as the Templo Mayor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3

The chapter is also a quiet contribution to the present book's argument that combat is one template among several for cosmic ordering rather than a universal or privileged one: the embedded Yu sidebar's two-templates framing (combat-ordering and channel-opening coexisting within the same Chinese tradition) is the chapter's main vehicle for that wider argument, and the sidebar's intra-traditional contrast feeds the present book's Part II discussion of the chaoskampf framing's reach and limits.footnote:[Per brief 21-gonggong.yaml special_instructions on the two-templates observation as the sidebar's load-bearing contribution; scope.md §"Book structure" Part II on the comparative chapters' role in articulating the chaoskampf framing's reach and limits; Lewis 2006, Yu chapters, on the channel-opening template.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Yu chapters

The chapter's overall reading of the Gonggong narrative is that the Han-period compilation-witnesses preserve a strongly-cosmogonic combat-and-repair sequence whose post-combat geography (tilted cosmos, eastward-flowing rivers) supplies one of the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases for the present book's Part II C1 argument, and whose intra-traditional pairing with the Yu hydraulic-ordering template supplies a structurally distinctive feature of the Chinese material that the chapter foregrounds within the wider comparative argument.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, chs. 2–3 (Gonggong) and Yu chapters, on the integrated reading of the Gonggong-and-Yu material as a strongly-cosmogonic combat-and-repair sequence paired with a hydraulic-ordering counter-template; per brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the chapter's principal contributions to Part II C1 and to the wider comparative argument.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=chs. 2-3; Yu chapters

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Marduk-Tiamat, Ch. 3) — Part II C1 cosmogonic-combat companion; both narratives generate the present cosmos out of or after the combat (Marduk builds sky and earth from Tiamat's body; Gonggong's combat tilts the cosmos and Nüwa repairs into the present asymmetric form); structural-only parallel across non-cognate traditions per Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 and Lambert 2013.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Sumer (Ninurta-Asag, Ch. 1) — Part II C1 cosmogonic-combat companion; Gonggong's water-releasing-through-broken-earth parallels Ninurta's post-combat reordering of the Tigris, with both narratives presenting a storm-or-water-god shaping present-day hydrography through combat; van Dijk 1983 *Lugal-e* and Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesoamerica (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec, Ch. 24) — Part II C1 cosmogonic-combat companion; both Gonggong and Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec are combat-cosmogonies with explicit geographical aetiology (Mount Buzhou; Templo Mayor as Coatepec); Matos Moctezuma 1988 and Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Watkins 1995 Part V; Part II C5) — the Gonggong combat is a non-Indo-European combat-cosmogony and is therefore not a Watkins-formula reflex; the chapter's role in the wider comparative argument is to show that the cosmogonic-combat type recurs across non-cognate traditions independently of the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT inheritance, supporting Part II C1's claim that combat-cosmogony is a structural-typological pattern rather than a uniquely Indo-European or Near-Eastern inheritance.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: China (Hou Yi, Ch. 22) — within-tradition follow-on chapter; Hou Yi's archery against the ten suns is the family-chapter companion to the Gonggong full chapter in the East Asian cluster, supplying a second early-imperial Chinese combat-narrative whose ordering-work (sun-shooting-and-monster-killing under sage-king Yao) is structurally distinct from the Gonggong cosmogony.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: China (Yu, Ch. 21 sidebar) — within-chapter sidebar; the Yu hydraulic-ordering template contrasts with the Gonggong combat-ordering template within the same Chinese tradition, and the intra-traditional contrast feeds the present book's Part II discussion of the chaoskampf framing's reach and limits.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Japan (Susanoo and Yamata no Orochi, Ch. 23) — East-Asian-cluster companion; Orochi's killing supplies a second East Asian combat-narrative whose chaoskampf framing is applied from outside (per Naumann / Isomae / Como), in contrast to the Gonggong narrative whose Han-period sources themselves present the combat as cosmogonic; the contrast is the East Asian cluster's principal internal methodological observation.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Methods sidebar (Ch. 33) — the Yu / Gonggong intra-traditional pairing of two ordering-templates within one tradition is a paradigmatic case study in the limits of the chaoskampf framing: the same Chinese tradition produces both a combat-cosmogony (Gonggong) and a hydraulic-ordering narrative (Yu) without the two contradicting each other, showing that combat is one template among several rather than a privileged or universal one.
```

findings:

  - id: FC-01
    severity: HIGH
    category: citation_wrong
    location: >
      §"Source overview" lines 55-56 and §"Cultural and ritual context" lines 181-182.
      Both EVIDENCE tags read "source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on non-Near-Eastern combat-myth."
      The two affected claims are: (1) "The chapter's general comparative anchor for the
      long-arc combat-myth tradition is Neil Forsyth's The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat
      Myth (Princeton University Press, 1987), whose treatment of non-Near-Eastern combat-myth
      supplies a wider scholarly anchor for the Gonggong narrative's place in the comparative
      dragon-combat corpus" (lines 55–56); and (2) the parallel restatement at lines 181–182
      ("Forsyth 1987's treatment of non-Near-Eastern combat-myth, which supplies a wider
      scholarly anchor for the Gonggong narrative's place in the comparative dragon-combat
      corpus").
    claim_text: >
      [Representative] "The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth
      tradition is Neil Forsyth's The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth (Princeton
      University Press, 1987), whose treatment of non-Near-Eastern combat-myth supplies a
      wider scholarly anchor for the Gonggong narrative's place in the comparative dragon-
      combat corpus and for the chapter's forward-link to Part II C1 of the present book."
    problem: >
      Forsyth 1987 "The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth" does not treat non-Near-Eastern
      combat myth in any substantive sense. The sources.yaml entry is explicit: the book
      "Traces the combat myth from Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic." Its scope is
      Near Eastern (Sumerian, Akkadian, Ugaritic) → Jewish → early Christian. The loc
      "on non-Near-Eastern combat-myth" is therefore a mislabel: Forsyth 1987 does not
      cover Chinese Gonggong material, does not treat the East Asian combat-myth tradition,
      and does not extend to the non-Near-Eastern corpus in the sense the claims document
      asserts. The book cannot serve as a comparative anchor for the Gonggong narrative's
      place in a wider "comparative dragon-combat corpus" that includes Chinese material —
      its coverage stops at Judeo-Christian apocalyptic. The characterisation of Forsyth
      1987 as a "general comparative anchor" for the Gonggong narrative specifically is
      misleading and the EVIDENCE loc tag is inaccurate.
    evidence: >
      sources.yaml entry for forsyth-1987: "Traces the combat myth from Sumer through
      Judeo-Christian apocalyptic. Full catalog-level treatment." Brief 21-gonggong.yaml
      lists forsyth-1987 as secondary source with loc "on non-Near-Eastern combat-myth" —
      this characterisation in the brief is inconsistent with Forsyth's documented scope,
      which is explicitly Near Eastern → Judeo-Christian, not East Asian. Trained knowledge
      confirms Forsyth 1987 does not include a chapter on Chinese cosmogonic combat.
    recommended_fix: >
      Remove or substantially qualify the Forsyth 1987 citations at lines 55-56 and 181-182.
      Forsyth 1987 is a legitimate secondary source for the combat-myth tradition in its
      Sumer-through-Judeo-Christian arc (and may be cited for claims about that arc's
      general typological context). It is not a valid anchor for "the Gonggong narrative's
      place in the comparative dragon-combat corpus" since the book does not treat Chinese
      material. The two sentences should either be rewritten to restrict Forsyth's role
      to the Near-Eastern / Judeo-Christian combat-myth background (the scope the book
      actually covers) or an alternative comparative source should be identified that
      genuinely treats cross-cultural combat-myth including East Asian cases. The EVIDENCE
      loc tag "on non-Near-Eastern combat-myth" must be corrected regardless.

  - id: FC-02
    severity: HIGH
    category: citation_wrong
    location: >
      §"Cultural and ritual context" line 178–179. The claim attributes to "Lewis 2006,
      The Flood Myths of Early China, chs. 2–3" the specific comparative placement of
      Gonggong alongside Marduk-Tiamat, Ninurta-Asag, and Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec
      as "the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases across non-cognate traditions."
    claim_text: >
      "The chapter's principal cross-cultural comparative companions, on Lewis 2006's
      framing and on the present book's Part II C1 organisation, are the Mesopotamian
      Marduk-Tiamat combat (chapter 3 of the present volume), the Sumerian Ninurta-Asag
      combat (chapter 1), and the Mesoamerican Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec combat
      (chapter 24 of the present volume): each is a combat-cosmogony in the strong sense
      in which the present features of the cosmos emerge from the combat's aftermath.
      [footnote: Lewis 2006 chs. 2–3 on the comparative-mythology placement of the
      Gonggong narrative alongside Marduk-Tiamat, Ninurta-Asag, and Huitzilopochtli-
      at-Coatepec]"
    problem: >
      Lewis 2006 "The Flood Myths of Early China" (SUNY Press) is a book about early
      Chinese flood and cosmogony myths. Its scope is Chinese — it does not make a
      comparative argument that places Gonggong alongside Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec
      (a Mesoamerican tradition) as one of four cosmogonic-combat cases. Lewis 2006
      chs. 2-3 may plausibly discuss comparative connections to Near Eastern flood and
      cosmogony traditions (a direction common in Chinese cosmogony scholarship), but
      the specific four-way grouping (Gonggong / Marduk / Ninurta / Huitzilopochtli)
      as "the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases" is the present book's own Part II
      C1 editorial framework — it is not something Lewis 2006 asserts. Attributing
      this four-way comparative placement to Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 with the phrase "on
      Lewis 2006's framing" is a false attribution: it presents the chapter's own
      classification as though it were Lewis's comparative claim. This is a scope-
      plausibility problem: Lewis 2006 (a Chinese-tradition specialist monograph)
      cannot plausibly supply the comparative warrant for a grouping that includes
      a Mesoamerican combat-myth.
    evidence: >
      sources.yaml entry for lewis-2006-flood: "Anchor for Gonggong and the Yu-sidebar."
      No comparative scope extending to Mesoamerica noted. Brief 21-gonggong.yaml
      comparative_hooks entry attributes the C1 pairing to "lewis-2006-flood; lambert-2013;
      matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor" — i.e., the Mesoamerican parallel is anchored in
      Matos Moctezuma 1988, not Lewis 2006. The claims document conflates the brief's
      composite comparative framework into a single Lewis 2006 attribution. Trained
      knowledge confirms Lewis 2006 does not treat Mesoamerican combat mythology.
    recommended_fix: >
      Revise the claim and its footnote to distinguish: (a) Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 for the
      Gonggong narrative's characterisation as a strongly-cosmogonic event; and (b) the
      present book's Part II C1 editorial framework as the source of the four-way grouping.
      The sentence should read something like: "The present book's Part II C1 organisation
      pairs the Gonggong narrative with [the three others] as the four clearest cosmogonic-
      combat cases across non-cognate traditions; Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 supplies the
      cosmogonic characterisation of the Gonggong narrative, while Matos Moctezuma 1988
      and Lambert 2013 anchor the Mesoamerican and Mesopotamian comparanda respectively."
      The EVIDENCE tag should not cite Lewis 2006 alone for the comparative placement.

  - id: FC-03
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Nüwa and the repair of the sky" lines 111-112. The claim about the five-coloured
      stones being "read in subsequent Han and post-Han Chinese cosmological writing as
      corresponding to the Five Phases or Five Elements (wuxing 五行)" is cited to
      "Birrell 1993, Chinese Mythology: An Introduction, chapters on cosmogony."
    claim_text: >
      "The five-coloured stones are presented in the Han sources as a technical-cosmological
      material rather than as a generic patch: their five colours are read in subsequent Han
      and post-Han Chinese cosmological writing as corresponding to the Five Phases or Five
      Elements (wuxing 五行) — wood, fire, earth, metal, water — that organise the
      cosmographic system within which the repair makes sense."
    problem: >
      The five-coloured-stones / wuxing Five Phases connection is a cosmological-system
      linkage made in secondary scholarship rather than an explicit reading found in the
      Han primary texts' own treatment of the Gonggong / Nüwa narrative. Birrell 1993's
      "chapters on cosmogony" covers the Gonggong-Nüwa narrative but may not specifically
      treat the five-coloured-stones-as-wuxing correspondence — the wuxing cosmological
      system is a Han cosmological framework that permeates many areas of Han culture,
      and the specific assertion that the five-coloured-stones in the Nüwa narrative are
      "read" as wuxing-coded is a specific secondary-scholarly claim whose presence in
      Birrell 1993's cosmogony chapters cannot be confirmed via trained knowledge at
      MEDIUM confidence. The wuxing connection is plausible but requires verification
      against Birrell's actual text, as Birrell 1993 may treat the five stones as a
      ritual-material detail without making the explicit wuxing correspondence claim.
    evidence: >
      Birrell 1993 covers the Gonggong-Nüwa narrative in its cosmogony chapters. The
      wuxing connection is plausible for a general Chinese mythology survey, but Birrell
      1993's cosmogony focus is on narrative content and cross-cultural comparison rather
      than on cosmological-system exegesis. The claim that this specific interpretive
      connection is present in Birrell's cosmogony chapters (rather than in a more
      specialised Han cosmological source) cannot be confirmed without access to the
      text. The content claim itself is substantively plausible (the wuxing connection
      is made in the secondary literature) but the specific attribution to Birrell 1993
      chapters on cosmogony is unverified.
    recommended_fix: >
      At human review, verify in Birrell 1993's cosmogony chapters whether the five-
      coloured-stones / wuxing correspondence is explicitly made or merely implied.
      If Birrell does not make the connection explicitly, the claim should either be
      softened ("their five colours may be connected with...") or a more specific
      Han cosmological source should be cited. If Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 treats the
      wuxing connection in passing, the EVIDENCE tag should be updated to include
      the Lewis citation as the primary anchor.

  - id: FC-04
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Setting and combatants" line 80. The claim attributes to "Lewis 2006, The Flood
      Myths of Early China, chs. 2-3" Nüwa's association "with the creation of humans
      (in some Han sources, by moulding humans from yellow earth)."
    claim_text: >
      "Nüwa (女媧), the serpent-tailed creator-goddess who repairs the broken sky after
      the combat, is one of the principal cosmogonic figures of the early-imperial Chinese
      pantheon and is associated more broadly with the creation of humans (in some Han
      sources, by moulding humans from yellow earth) and with the mending and ordering
      of the cosmos."
      [footnote cites Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 AND Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony]
    problem: >
      Lewis 2006 "The Flood Myths of Early China" is specifically about flood myths.
      Its chapters 2-3 treat the Gonggong / Nüwa broken-pillar cosmogony and the
      flood-and-repair sequence. Nüwa's role in the human-creation-from-yellow-earth
      tradition is a separate cosmogonic sub-tradition (attested in the Fengsu tongyi
      and other sources) that is not principally a flood-myth topic. Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3
      may note Nüwa's wider cosmogonic associations in passing, but using Lewis 2006 as
      an anchor for the human-creation detail specifically (rather than Birrell 1993,
      which covers Nüwa's full cosmogonic portfolio in its mythology-survey chapters)
      is a scope-plausibility concern. The Birrell 1993 co-citation in the same footnote
      is the more appropriate anchor for the human-creation detail. This is LOW-to-MEDIUM
      severity because Birrell 1993 is already cited and the content is correct; the
      concern is that Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 is listed first in the footnote as though it
      were the primary source for the human-creation detail.
    evidence: >
      Lewis 2006 documented scope in sources.yaml: "Anchor for Gonggong and the Yu-sidebar."
      No human-creation material mentioned. Birrell 1993 documented scope: "General Chinese
      reference." Nüwa's human-creation tradition is the broader cosmogonic material that
      a mythology survey (Birrell) would cover; a flood-myths specialist monograph (Lewis)
      focuses on the flood-and-cosmogony cluster. The human-creation-from-yellow-earth
      detail is plausible and attested; the citation ordering in the footnote may imply
      Lewis 2006 is the primary support for this detail when Birrell 1993 is the
      more appropriate anchor.
    recommended_fix: >
      Reorder the footnote to cite Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony as the primary
      anchor for Nüwa's wider creation-and-mending role (including the yellow-earth
      human-creation), with Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 as the anchor specifically for the
      post-combat repair role in the flood-and-cosmogony sequence. At human review,
      confirm whether Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 explicitly mentions the yellow-earth
      human-creation detail; if not, remove Lewis from this footnote's human-creation
      claim or add a qualifier ("Lewis 2006 on Nüwa's repair role; Birrell 1993 on
      Nüwa's wider cosmogonic associations including human creation").

  - id: FC-05
    severity: LOW
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Source overview" lines 36-38. The claim about the Liezi's date and framing
      cites Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 for the characterisation of the Liezi as "3rd–4th
      century CE but drawing on earlier material."
    claim_text: >
      "The Liezi (列子, 'Master Lie'), a heterogeneous Daoist compilation in its received
      form conventionally dated to the 3rd–4th century CE but drawing on earlier material,
      supplies a further later witness to the Gonggong / Nüwa cosmogony with characteristic
      Daoist-philosophical framing of the combat-and-repair sequence as a cosmological
      aetiology."
      [footnote cites Lewis 2006 chs. 2-3 and Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony]
    problem: >
      The Liezi's conventional date (3rd–4th century CE in its received form) and the
      "drawing on earlier material" characterisation are well-established in the secondary
      literature. The more specific claim — that the Liezi's treatment of the Gonggong /
      Nüwa cosmogony has a "characteristic Daoist-philosophical framing" — is cited to
      Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony as the specific source for this framing
      characterisation. Birrell 1993 is a plausible anchor for this claim. However,
      the specific framing assessment (Daoist-philosophical as opposed to cosmological-
      aetiology in a non-Daoist sense) is an interpretive characterisation that is
      attributed to Birrell without a specific chapter or page, making it unverifiable
      at the chapter-level citation discipline. This is LOW severity because the content
      is substantively plausible (the Liezi has a distinctively Daoist framing in its
      received form) and the citation anchor (Birrell 1993 chapters on cosmogony) is
      appropriate; the concern is that the specific "Daoist-philosophical framing"
      characterisation is an interpretive claim that needs to be confirmed as present in
      Birrell's cosmogony chapters rather than being an inference from other reading.
    evidence: >
      The Liezi's date and Daoist character are confirmed via trained knowledge of Chinese
      textual history — the 3rd–4th century CE date for the received Liezi is standard.
      The Daoist-philosophical framing of cosmogonic material in the Liezi is consistent
      with trained knowledge of the text. Birrell 1993 as a general Chinese mythology
      survey plausibly discusses the Liezi's cosmogonic passages. The content is not
      doubted; the citability at chapter-only level is the concern.
    recommended_fix: >
      At human review, verify in Birrell 1993's cosmogony chapters whether the Liezi's
      Daoist-philosophical framing is explicitly characterised as such. If present,
      the citation is appropriate. If Birrell treats the Liezi material without the
      "Daoist-philosophical" qualifier, soften the claim to "characteristic cosmological-
      aetiological framing" or add a note that the Daoist framing characterisation is
      based on the Liezi's known Daoist context rather than on a specific Birrell passage.

  - claim_number: 1

      period (5th–3rd c. BCE); the surviving narrative is preserved in Han-dynasty compilations.

    source_loc: chs. 2-3

      (absence of a continuous Warring States primary text) is confirmed via trained knowledge
      of early Chinese textual history and is consistent with Lewis 2006's documented scope as
      an anchor for the Gonggong material.

  - claim_number: 2

      (c. 139 BCE), associated with Liu An, Prince of Huainan, whose chapter 天文訓 preserves
      the fullest single account.
    source_id: lewis-2006-flood
    source_loc: chs. 2-3

      field. Lewis 2006 is the appropriate anchor for these textual-witness claims.

# Input file: chapters/22-hou-yi.claims.adoc
