```
[[ch-22-hou-yi-claims]]
== Hou Yi and the Suns — Claims

// One fact per paragraph; chapter-draft will transform this into narrative.
// Short full chapter (related-tier) treating a single Chinese hero-cycle:
// the archer Hou Yi shoots down nine of the ten suns and slays a series
// of monstrous beasts to restore order under the sage-king Yao. Per
// brief 22-hou-yi.yaml, the chapter is "related (short full)" and
// targets ~3500 narrative words. Variant handling is co-equal across
// the three principal early-Chinese textual witnesses (Chuci 'Tian wen',
// Huainanzi, Shanhaijing) treated as a composite-editorial cluster with
// Lewis 2006 as the scholarly synthesis.

=== Source overview

The chapter treats the Chinese hero-narrative of Hou Yi, the archer who shoots down nine of ten scorching suns and slays a sequence of monstrous beasts under the sage-king Yao, presented in the present book as a related-tier (short full) chapter rounding out the East Asian cluster by exhibiting a different Chinese combat-pattern from the cosmogonic Gonggong combat of the preceding chapter.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml on the related-tier (short full) classification and on the chapter's role of rounding out the East Asian cluster with a non-cosmogonic Chinese combat-pattern; Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China*, treatment of the Hou Yi / Yao cluster as a distinct Chinese combat-narrative tradition from the cosmogonic Gonggong material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The Hou Yi material has no single continuous primary-source witness in any early Chinese text: the narrative as it is read in modern scholarship is a composite reconstructed from several Warring States and Han-period sources that each preserve different elements of the cycle, and the chapter's textual basis is therefore composite-editorial rather than single-text-anchored, in line with the brief's variant classification.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.classification: co-equal; variants.summary on the composite-editorial character of the Hou Yi material across Chuci 'Tian wen', the Huainanzi, and the Shanhaijing; Lewis 2006, treatment of the Hou Yi / Yao cluster as a multi-source composite within early Chinese mythological literature.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The earliest of the principal textual witnesses is the *Chuci* ("Songs of Chu") poem *Tian wen* ("Heavenly Questions"), conventionally dated to approximately the third century BCE and preserved in the wider *Chuci* anthology associated with the southern state of Chu, whose catechetic question-format alludes to Hou Yi and the suns within a longer sequence of cryptic questions about gods, heroes, and cosmic events.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.summary on *Chuci* 'Tian wen' as c. 3rd c. BCE textual witness for the Hou Yi material; Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, ch. on solar myths, on the *Tian wen*'s catechetic-question form and its place within the *Chuci* anthology associated with the southern state of Chu.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The fullest single early-Chinese narrative account of the Hou Yi suns-and-beasts cycle is preserved in the *Huainanzi*, a philosophical compendium composed at the court of Liu An, king of Huainan, in the second century BCE and presenting the Hou Yi material within a wider syncretic cosmological-and-political programme that draws on earlier Warring States traditions.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.summary on the *Huainanzi* (2nd c. BCE) as the principal narrative-form textual witness for the Hou Yi cycle; Lewis 2006, treatment of the *Huainanzi* as the most narratively-developed early Chinese source for the suns-and-beasts cycle; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the *Huainanzi*'s second-century-BCE compositional context at the court of Liu An.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The *Shanhaijing* ("Classic of Mountains and Seas"), an early Chinese geographical-and-mythographic compendium of layered date (with strata reaching from the Warring States through the early Han period), supplies the principal early-Chinese witness for the catalogue of monstrous beasts that Hou Yi confronts and for the geographical placement of the beasts within a fantastical cartography of mountains, rivers, and outer-zones.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.summary on the *Shanhaijing* as the third principal textual witness for the Hou Yi cycle; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the *Shanhaijing*'s mythographic-geographical-compendium form and its layered Warring States and Han-period strata; Lewis 2006 on the *Shanhaijing*'s placement of the Hou Yi beasts within the wider Chinese mythological cartography.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The three principal textual witnesses preserve different elements of the cycle with different emphases: the *Chuci* 'Tian wen' alludes to the sun-shooting in catechetic-question form without narrating the episode; the *Huainanzi* gives the narrative-form account of the ten suns and the commission to Hou Yi together with the beast-slaying sequence; and the *Shanhaijing* supplies the catalogue of monstrous adversaries and the geographical placement of the encounters, with no single source preserving the complete cycle as a connected narrative.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.summary on the differential preservation of cycle-elements across the three textual witnesses and on the absence of a single complete-narrative source; Lewis 2006, treatment of the Hou Yi cluster as a multi-source composite where the three principal witnesses preserve distinct cycle-elements; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same multi-source pattern within early Chinese mythological literature.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's principal scholarly anchor is Mark Edward Lewis's *The Flood Myths of Early China*, published by SUNY Press in 2006, which treats the Hou Yi / Yao cluster as one of the early-Chinese order-restoring narrative complexes alongside the Gonggong cosmogonic combat of the preceding chapter and supplies the present chapter's working synthesis across the *Chuci*, *Huainanzi*, and *Shanhaijing* witnesses.footnote:[Lewis 2006, *The Flood Myths of Early China* (Albany: SUNY Press; ISBN 9780791466636), treatment of the Hou Yi / Yao cluster as an order-restoring narrative complex within early Chinese mythological literature; per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml sources.primary on Lewis 2006 as the principal scholarly anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's secondary scholarly anchor is Anne Birrell's *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction*, published by Johns Hopkins University Press in 1993, whose chapter on solar myths supplies the principal English-language synoptic survey of the Hou Yi material and treats the archer-hero within the wider context of Chinese solar-mythology and the Yao-era ordering narratives.footnote:[Birrell 1993, *Chinese Mythology: An Introduction* (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press; ISBN 9780801849954), ch. on solar myths, on Hou Yi within the wider context of Chinese solar-mythology and Yao-era ordering narratives; per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml sources.primary on Birrell 1993 as the secondary scholarly anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

Both Lewis 2006 and Birrell 1993 are printed-only English-language scholarly anchors and are cited at chapter level in the present chapter — Lewis 2006 at the level of the Hou Yi / Yao cluster discussion, Birrell 1993 at the level of the chapter on solar myths — in line with the citation discipline applied across the present book's related-tier chapters that use a small number of secondary scholarly anchors as their textual basis.footnote:[Lewis 2006 and Birrell 1993, both printed only and cited at chapter level in the present chapter per the established citation discipline; per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml on the two-source primary-anchor structure of the chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter is a related-tier (short full) treatment with a target of approximately 3,500 narrative words, organising the Hou Yi cycle into two distinguishable but co-occurring narrative components — the ten-suns episode and the monster-beasts sequence — both of which the chapter presents while noting their relation, and presenting the cycle in its order-restoring rather than cosmogonic register, since the cosmos pre-exists the combat in this material and Hou Yi's role is to restore order within an already-existing world rather than to establish the cosmos in the first place.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml on the related-tier (short full) classification, the 3,500-word target, the two-component structure (ten-suns episode and monster-beasts sequence), and the order-restoring (rather than cosmogonic) framing that distinguishes the Hou Yi cycle from the preceding chapter's Gonggong cosmogonic combat; Lewis 2006 on the order-restoring character of the Hou Yi material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's framing premise is that Hou Yi is a related-tier rather than core-tier case for the present book's chaoskampf typology: the suns and the monstrous beasts are not a single cosmogonic adversary in the manner of Tiamat, Vṛtra, or Tiamat's structural counterparts in other CORE chapters, and the cycle's order-making work is heroic-restorative rather than world-establishing, with the cosmos and the human social order both already in place under the sage-king Yao when the suns rise together.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's heroic-restorative (rather than cosmogonic) framing and on the related-tier classification it warrants; Lewis 2006 on the place of the Hou Yi cycle within Yao-era narratives of order-restoration; scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the related-tier criteria where the cosmogonic-combat-work test is weaker.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C5 (the comparative chapter on the Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula) only peripherally, since Hou Yi is not an Indo-European reflex and the cycle's contribution to that comparative argument is by way of cross-cultural breadth rather than as evidence for the IE formula itself; the chapter's principal back-link is to the preceding chapter on Gonggong, with which the present chapter shares the East Asian geographical cluster but contrasts in combat-typology (cosmogonic vs. order-restoring).footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml special_instructions on the peripheral-only contribution to Part II C5 and on the cross-cultural-breadth role of the chapter; Lewis 2006 on the contrast between the Gonggong cosmogonic-combat narrative and the Hou Yi order-restoring narrative within the same early Chinese mythological tradition; scope.md §"Book structure" on the East Asian geographic-cluster ordering that places Hou Yi after Gonggong.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

=== Setting: the Yao era and the ten suns

The narrative's chronological setting in early-Chinese mytho-historical time is the era of the sage-king Yao, one of the legendary pre-dynastic sovereigns whose reign-period in the conventional Chinese mytho-chronology precedes the Xia, Shang, and Zhou dynasties and is associated in the textual tradition with the establishment of the proper ordering of human society and the regulation of the calendar, the seasons, and the cosmos.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the chronological placement of the Hou Yi narrative in the Yao era within the conventional Chinese mytho-chronology of pre-dynastic sage-kings; Lewis 2006 on Yao's place at the head of the legendary pre-dynastic sovereigns associated with the establishment of social, calendrical, and cosmic order.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The narrative's pre-existing cosmic order is significant for the chapter's classification within the present book's typology: Hou Yi acts within an already-ordered cosmos under an already-instituted sage-king, and the crisis the cycle addresses is a perturbation of that order rather than the absence of it — the suns-crisis and the monster-beasts crisis are disturbances within an existing system that need to be removed, not gaps in a not-yet-established system that need to be filled.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's order-restoring rather than cosmogonic framing; Lewis 2006 on the pre-existing-cosmic-order setting of the Yao-era narratives within early Chinese mythology; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same pre-existing-order setting.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The narrative's solar premise is that there are ten suns rather than one: the early Chinese textual tradition preserves a mythological cosmology in which ten suns, one for each day of a ten-day week, normally rise in succession from a mulberry tree (the Fusang tree) at the eastern edge of the world, with one sun emerging each day and the other nine remaining in the tree, and the suns' regular alternation supplies the daily and weekly rhythm of cosmic time.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the ten-suns mythological cosmology, the Fusang mulberry tree at the eastern edge of the world, and the regular daily alternation of the suns within the early Chinese textual tradition; Lewis 2006 on the same ten-suns cosmology as the narrative premise of the Hou Yi cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The ten suns are presented in some strata of the early Chinese textual tradition as the children of the high god Di Jun and his consort Xihe, the sun-mother who bathes the suns at the eastern edge of the world before each one's daily rising, with the divine parentage placing the suns within a wider mythological pantheon of cosmic deities and legitimating their place in the regular celestial order.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the divine parentage of the ten suns as children of Di Jun and Xihe within the early Chinese textual tradition and on Xihe's role as sun-mother bathing the suns at the eastern edge of the world; Lewis 2006 on the same divine-parentage tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The narrative's precipitating crisis is that the ten suns, contrary to the regular daily alternation, on a single occasion rise together: all ten leave the Fusang tree at once and appear simultaneously in the sky, and the resulting heat scorches the earth, dries the rivers, withers the crops, and exposes both the natural environment and human society to a catastrophic disturbance of the proper cosmic order.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the precipitating crisis in which the ten suns rise together against the regular alternation, with the consequent scorching of the earth, the drying of rivers, and the withering of crops within the early Chinese textual tradition; Lewis 2006 on the same precipitating crisis within the Hou Yi narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The crisis's character is environmental-and-political rather than cosmogonic-and-foundational: the existing world is at risk of destruction by overheating rather than not-yet-existing-and-needing-to-be-formed, and the political order under Yao is at risk of collapse because the climatic perturbation makes human existence within the king's domain unsustainable, with the framing aligning the Hou Yi cycle with the present book's "order under threat" subtype rather than with the cosmogonic-combat subtype.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml on the cosmological function of the Hou Yi cycle as solar-plurality rectification and environmental restoration; Lewis 2006 on the environmental-and-political character of the suns-crisis within early Chinese narratives of Yao-era order-restoration; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same environmental-political framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

=== Hou Yi and the shooting of the suns

The figure of Hou Yi is introduced into the narrative as a divine archer of unparalleled skill: in the strata of the textual tradition that preserve his celestial origin, Hou Yi is sent down from the heavens by the high god Di Jun (the father of the ten suns themselves) to deal with the crisis caused by his own children, and the commission places the archer in the position of a divine agent whose authority for the killing comes from the heavens rather than from the human sage-king alone.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on Hou Yi's celestial origin and on his commission by Di Jun to address the suns-crisis caused by Di Jun's own children within the early Chinese textual tradition; Lewis 2006 on the divine-agent framing of Hou Yi within the Yao-era narratives.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The archer's principal attribute is his bow, with which he is equipped — in the textual strata that supply this detail — by Di Jun himself at the moment of his commission, with red corded arrows in some accounts; the bow-and-arrows are the narrative's load-bearing combat-implement and the archery-as-method is what defines the cycle's characteristic combat-pattern as distinct from the close-combat patterns of other order-restoring narratives in the present book.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on Hou Yi's bow as the load-bearing combat-implement of the cycle and on the red corded arrows tradition within the early Chinese textual material; Lewis 2006 on the archery-as-method that defines the Hou Yi cycle's combat-pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The act of shooting the suns is presented in the *Huainanzi* and the wider tradition as a sequential drawing-and-loosing of the bow against the simultaneously-risen suns: Hou Yi shoots one sun, then the next, then the next, with each arrow striking its mark and bringing down a sun in turn, until nine of the ten suns have been shot and only one remains in the sky.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the sequential sun-shooting sequence in the *Huainanzi* and the wider tradition with nine of the ten suns brought down in turn; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same sequential pattern of the suns-shooting within the early Chinese textual material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The narrative's nine-of-ten outcome is significant for the cycle's classification within the present book's combat-typology: Hou Yi is not eliminating the suns as a class — the sun as a celestial body remains in the sky and continues to provide the daily light and warmth that the cosmos requires — but is correcting an excess, restoring the proper one-sun-at-a-time alternation that the Fusang-tree cosmology presupposes, with the killing-arithmetic itself encoding the order-restoration logic of the cycle.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the order-restoration logic encoded in the nine-of-ten killing-arithmetic of the Hou Yi cycle and on the contrast with class-elimination patterns in other combat-narratives; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the post-shooting cosmos in which the surviving single sun continues the regular daily cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

[VARIANT: primary=the *Huainanzi* (2nd c. BCE) preserves the narrative-form account of Hou Yi shooting nine of the ten simultaneously-risen suns at Yao's request and in commission from Di Jun, with the nine fallen suns leaving one remaining in the sky | alt=the *Chuci* 'Tian wen' (c. 3rd c. BCE) alludes to the sun-shooting only in catechetic-question form without narrating the episode; the *Shanhaijing* preserves the geographical placement of the encounters and the catalogue of associated beasts but does not supply the connected narrative of the sun-shooting itself | chosen: present-the-Huainanzi-narrative-skeleton | reason: per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.classification: co-equal with composite-editorial handling and Lewis 2006 as the scholarly synthesis; the *Huainanzi* is the fullest single narrative-form witness for the suns-episode while the *Chuci* and the *Shanhaijing* preserve allusive and catalogue-form material respectively.]

The killing of the nine suns immediately ends the scorching crisis: with only one sun remaining in the sky, the proper daily alternation is in principle restored, the temperature returns to a survivable level, the rivers begin to recover, and the human order under Yao is preserved from collapse, with the archery-act functioning as a single decisive intervention that resolves the environmental-and-political emergency.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the resolution of the scorching crisis through the killing of the nine suns and the restoration of the proper daily alternation within the early Chinese narrative tradition; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the post-shooting recovery of the environmental and human order under Yao.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

=== The monstrous beasts

The Hou Yi cycle's second narrative component is the sequence of monstrous-beast slayings that the archer carries out either after or in parallel with the killing of the suns, with the textual tradition presenting the beast-slayings as a series of further order-restoring acts that address other disturbances of the proper cosmic and human order beyond the suns-crisis itself.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the monstrous-beast sequence as the second narrative component of the Hou Yi cycle and on its order-restoring function within the early Chinese narrative tradition; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the relation between the suns-episode and the beast-slayings within the cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The principal early-Chinese textual witnesses do not preserve a single fixed list of the monstrous beasts: different sources name different combinations, with the *Huainanzi* preserving the most narratively-developed enumeration and the *Shanhaijing* supplying the geographical placement and the iconographic descriptions of the individual beasts within its wider mythographic-cartographical compendium; the present chapter accordingly follows Lewis 2006's synthesis of the beast-catalogue across the witnesses.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the synthesis of the monstrous-beast catalogue across the early-Chinese textual witnesses with the *Huainanzi* as the principal narrative-form source and the *Shanhaijing* as the principal geographical-and-iconographic source; per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.summary on the variation in the nine-beasts subnarrative across the textual witnesses.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

[VARIANT: primary=Lewis 2006's synthesis of the *Huainanzi*'s narratively-developed beast-enumeration and the *Shanhaijing*'s geographical-and-iconographic catalogue of the beasts associated with Hou Yi | alt=individual textual witnesses preserve different combinations of the beasts with differing names and differing geographical placements; no single source preserves the same complete list as another | chosen: present-the-Lewis-synthesis-skeleton | reason: per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.classification: co-equal with Lewis 2006 as the scholarly synthesis across the *Chuci*, *Huainanzi*, and *Shanhaijing* witnesses; per brief variants.summary on the textual variation in the nine-beasts subnarrative.]

One of the named beasts in the cycle is the Chiseltooth (Chinese *Zaochi*), a tusked monstrous figure whose name in the early-Chinese tradition refers to a chisel-like protruding tooth and who is presented as one of the order-disturbing beasts that Hou Yi confronts and kills in the course of the wider beast-slaying sequence.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the Chiseltooth (Chinese *Zaochi*) as one of the named monstrous beasts that Hou Yi confronts and kills in the early-Chinese narrative tradition; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same Chiseltooth figure with the chisel-like-tooth iconography that gives the figure its name.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

A second of the named beasts is the Nine-headed Monster (Chinese *Jiuying*), a multi-headed creature whose multi-headedness is the iconographic marker of its monstrous status within the early-Chinese mythological tradition and whose killing by Hou Yi forms one of the most structurally-significant of the beast-slayings for the cycle's chaoskampf-comparative reading.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the Nine-headed Monster (Chinese *Jiuying*) as one of the named monstrous beasts that Hou Yi confronts and kills, with the multi-headedness as the iconographic marker of monstrous status; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same multi-headed-monster figure within the cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

A third of the named beasts is the Zhuyin serpent (sometimes alternatively named *Xiushe* or *Bashe* in different strata of the textual tradition), a giant serpentine figure whose serpentine morphology and large size place it within the cross-tradition family of monstrous serpents that hero-figures across many of the present book's chapters confront, although the early-Chinese material's treatment of the serpent is heroic-combat-pattern rather than cosmogonic-combat-pattern.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the Zhuyin serpent (with the alternative names *Xiushe* and *Bashe* in different strata of the textual tradition) as one of the named monstrous beasts that Hou Yi confronts and kills; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same giant-serpent figure within the cycle and on the heroic-combat-pattern character of the killing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The early-Chinese tradition's wider beast-catalogue for the Hou Yi cycle includes additional figures named in the *Huainanzi* and in the *Shanhaijing* — among them a wind-causing giant bird, a giant boar, and the figure named *Yayu* — with the precise combination of beasts varying across the textual witnesses and the present chapter naming the Chiseltooth, the Nine-headed Monster, and the Zhuyin serpent as the three brief-named representatives of the wider sequence.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the wider beast-catalogue across the *Huainanzi* and the *Shanhaijing* including the wind-causing giant bird, the giant boar, and the figure named *Yayu* as additional members of the sequence; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same wider beast-catalogue and on its variation across the textual witnesses; per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml summary on the three brief-named representatives (Chiseltooth, Nine-headed Monster, Zhuyin serpent).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The geographical placement of the beast-encounters in the *Shanhaijing* mythographic-cartographical apparatus locates each killing in a specific named region of the fantastical early-Chinese cartography, with the encounters distributed across the four directional zones of the world and the killings together constituting a comprehensive sweep across the geography of the inhabited cosmos that complements the celestial sweep of the suns-shooting in the same cycle.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the *Shanhaijing*'s geographical placement of the beast-encounters across the four directional zones of the early-Chinese mythological cartography and on the geographical-comprehensiveness pattern that the placement encodes; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same geographical-distribution pattern of the encounters.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading that the celestial sweep of the suns-shooting and the geographical sweep of the beast-killings together constitute a comprehensive cosmic-and-terrestrial order-restoration pattern is an editorial synthesis of Lewis 2006's treatment of the cycle rather than a single source-attested formulation; Lewis 2006 surveys the suns-episode and the beast-sequence as the two narrative components of the cycle without supplying this exact celestial-and-terrestrial-comprehensive-sweep phrasing, and the synthesis is offered for transparency. | basis: Lewis 2006 treats the suns-episode and the beast-sequence as both order-restoring components of a single Hou Yi cycle, and the *Shanhaijing*'s geographical apparatus places the beast-encounters across the directional zones of the cartography. | risk: alternative readings are possible — a stronger reading would treat the two components as more loosely connected episodes within a wider Yao-era narrative-cluster; the chapter's celestial-plus-terrestrial reading is a reasonable but not the only available synthesis.]

The killing-method varies across the beast-slayings: in some encounters Hou Yi uses his bow and arrows in the same archery-pattern as in the suns-shooting; in others he uses close-combat weapons; the variation in killing-method across the beast-sequence distinguishes the Hou Yi cycle from sequences in other traditions in which the hero uses a single signature weapon throughout, although the bow-and-arrows remain the cycle's iconic implement.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the variation in killing-method across the Hou Yi beast-sequence with archery and close-combat patterns both attested in the early-Chinese textual witnesses; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the bow-and-arrows as the cycle's iconic implement despite the variation in method across individual beast-encounters.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The narrative outcome of the beast-sequence is the comprehensive removal of the named monstrous adversaries from the inhabited zones of the Yao-era cosmos: with the beasts killed and the suns reduced to one, the environmental-and-political crisis that the cycle had presented as its precipitating problem is fully resolved, and the proper Yao-era order is restored across both the celestial and the terrestrial dimensions of the world.footnote:[Lewis 2006 on the comprehensive resolution of the environmental-and-political crisis through the combined suns-shooting and beast-slaying sequences within the Hou Yi cycle; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the post-cycle restoration of the proper Yao-era order.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

=== Aftermath: the relation between the components and Hou Yi's later fate

The relation between the suns-episode and the beast-sequence is one of co-occurrence rather than strict dependence within the early-Chinese textual tradition: the two components are routinely presented together in the principal witnesses (most prominently in the *Huainanzi*'s narrative account) but are also distinguishable as separate order-restoring acts, with each component being independently capable of being treated as a free-standing episode within the wider Yao-era narrative-cluster.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's task of presenting both components and noting their relation; Lewis 2006 on the co-occurrence-rather-than-strict-dependence relation between the suns-episode and the beast-sequence in the *Huainanzi* and the wider tradition; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same distinguishable-but-co-occurring relation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The early-Chinese textual tradition preserves further narrative material about Hou Yi's later fate beyond the suns-and-beasts cycle proper: in some strata Hou Yi receives the elixir of immortality from the Queen Mother of the West (Xiwangmu), the elixir is taken by his wife Chang'e who flees with it to the moon, and Hou Yi himself remains on earth in a subsequent narrative arc that the textual tradition develops in different directions in different sources.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the Hou Yi / Chang'e / Queen Mother of the West narrative arc beyond the suns-and-beasts cycle proper within the early-Chinese textual tradition; Lewis 2006 on the same later-fate material as a separate narrative-cluster from the order-restoring cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The Chang'e and elixir-of-immortality material is treated in the present chapter as adjacent to but separable from the order-restoring suns-and-beasts cycle that is the chapter's principal focus, with the chapter's brief-defined scope concentrated on the suns-and-beasts material as the chaoskampf-relevant component and the Chang'e narrative-arc not developed in the present chapter beyond this brief acknowledgement of its presence in the wider tradition.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml summary on the chapter's focus on the suns-and-beasts cycle as the chaoskampf-relevant component; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the separability of the Chang'e narrative-arc from the order-restoring cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

The Hou Yi cycle's place within the present book's chaoskampf typology is as a heroic order-restoration narrative whose combat-pattern is multi-adversary-sequential rather than single-adversary-decisive: where Marduk confronts Tiamat and Indra confronts Vṛtra in single decisive cosmogonic-or-sovereignty combats, Hou Yi confronts a series of distinct adversaries across both the celestial and the terrestrial dimensions of the world, and the cycle's order-making work is accomplished through cumulative removal of multiple disturbing presences rather than through a single dispatch of a paramount opponent.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml cultural_relevance on the heroic-restorative function of the cycle; Lewis 2006 on the multi-adversary-sequential combat-pattern of the Hou Yi cycle and its contrast with single-adversary-decisive patterns in other combat-narrative traditions; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same multi-adversary structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The cycle's combat-pattern is structurally closer to the Greek Herakles labours sequence (chapter 12 of the present volume on Greek dragon-combats after Zeus-Typhon, which treats Herakles among the wider family of Greek dragon-slayers) than to the cosmogonic-combat patterns of the present book's CORE chapters: in both the Hou Yi cycle and the Herakles labours, a hero-figure works through a sequence of monstrous adversaries whose individual killings together accomplish the cycle's order-making work, with no single adversary functioning as the paramount cosmogonic opponent.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml cross_cultural_parallels on the structural parallel between the Hou Yi beast-slaying cluster and the Herakles labours of chapter 12; Lewis 2006 on the multi-adversary-sequential combat-pattern of the Hou Yi cycle; Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the same structural pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's contrast with the preceding chapter on Gonggong is therefore both structural and typological: where the Gonggong chapter treats a single decisive cosmogonic combat in which the present features of the cosmos (the asymmetric tilt of heaven and earth, the eastward flow of rivers) emerge from the combat's aftermath, the present chapter treats a multi-stage heroic order-restoration in which the cosmos pre-exists the combat and is preserved-rather-than-established by it; the two chapters together exhibit two distinct Chinese combat-templates within a single tradition.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's role of showing a different Chinese combat-pattern from Gonggong; Lewis 2006 on the contrast between the cosmogonic Gonggong narrative and the order-restoring Hou Yi narrative within the early-Chinese mythological tradition; brief 21-gonggong.yaml on the cosmogonic-combat character of the Gonggong material that the present chapter contrasts with.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's contribution to the present book's wider comparative argument is by way of cross-cultural breadth rather than as a load-bearing exemplar of any single typological subtype: Hou Yi is not a cosmogonic-combat case, not a sovereignty-combat case in the strong sense (Yao's sovereignty pre-exists the combat and is preserved rather than established by it), not a cyclical-maintenance case (the cycle is once-only rather than re-enacted), and not an eschatological-combat case; the cycle's contribution to the book is in showing what an order-restoring heroic combat-narrative looks like as a non-cosmogonic Chinese counterpoint to the Gonggong material.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's cross-cultural-breadth contribution rather than load-bearing-exemplar role; scope.md §"Subject definition" on the four typological subtypes (cosmogonic, sovereignty, cyclical-maintenance, eschatological); Lewis 2006 on the order-restoring rather than order-establishing function of the Hou Yi cycle.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C5 (the comparative chapter on the Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula) only peripherally, since Hou Yi is not an Indo-European reflex and the Chinese material's contribution to the IE-formula argument is by way of typological-breadth comparanda rather than as evidence for the formula itself; the chapter's secondary forward-link is to Part II C2 (combat as sovereignty), where the Yao-era order-restoration provides a comparandum-by-contrast to the sovereignty-establishing combats of Marduk, Baal, and Indra.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml special_instructions on the peripheral-only contribution to Part II C5 and on the chapter's cross-cultural-breadth role; scope.md §"Book structure" on the Part II thematic comparative chapters including C5 (IE formula) and C2 (combat as sovereignty); Lewis 2006 on the contrast between the order-restoring Hou Yi material and the sovereignty-establishing combats of other traditions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The chapter's principal back-link within the present book is to the immediately preceding Chapter 21 on Gonggong, with which the present chapter shares the East Asian geographic cluster and the early-Chinese textual-source culture but contrasts in combat-typology (cosmogonic vs. order-restoring); the chapter's secondary back-link is to Chapter 12 (the family chapter on Greek dragon-combats after Zeus-Typhon), with which the present chapter shares the multi-adversary-sequential combat-pattern despite the radical difference in cultural and textual context.footnote:[Per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml cross_cultural_parallels on the back-link to chapter 12 (Herakles labours) and on the contrast with chapter 21 (Gonggong); scope.md §"Book structure" on the East Asian geographic-cluster ordering that places the present chapter immediately after Gonggong and within the wider sequence of related-tier chapters that follow the CORE-chapter sequence on Greek combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster

The Hou Yi cycle's cultural-historical importance within the early-Chinese mythological tradition is significant within its own register: the suns-shooting and the beast-slayings are routinely cited in early-Chinese philosophical, political, and literary texts as exemplary acts of heroic intervention on behalf of the cosmic-and-political order, and the figure of Hou Yi has remained a load-bearing reference within Chinese literary, scholarly, and popular culture from the Warring States period onwards even though the present chapter treats the cycle at related-tier rather than core-tier within the chaoskampf typology.footnote:[Birrell 1993, ch. on solar myths, on the cultural-historical importance of the Hou Yi cycle within early-Chinese philosophical, political, and literary texts and on the figure's continuing reference-role within Chinese literary culture from the Warring States period onwards; Lewis 2006 on the same cultural-historical importance.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=birrell-1993 ; loc=ch. on solar myths

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Greece (Herakles labours, Ch. 12) — multi-adversary-sequential combat-pattern in which a hero-figure works through a series of monstrous opponents to accomplish cumulative order-making work; both cycles present heroic rather than cosmogonic combat-patterns within their respective traditions; per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml cross_cultural_parallels.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: China (Gonggong, Ch. 21) — preceding-chapter East Asian companion in the present book's geographic-cluster ordering; structural and typological contrast between the cosmogonic Gonggong combat (the cosmos's present features emerge from the combat) and the order-restoring Hou Yi cycle (the cosmos pre-exists the combat and is preserved by it); two distinct Chinese combat-templates within a single tradition.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Marduk-Tiamat, Ch. 3) and India (Indra-Vṛtra, Ch. 14) — single-adversary-decisive combat-patterns that contrast with the Hou Yi cycle's multi-adversary-sequential pattern; comparandum-by-contrast for Part II C2 (combat as sovereignty) where Yao's pre-existing sovereignty is preserved rather than established by the cycle.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Indo-European HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Watkins 1995 Part V; Part II C5) — Hou Yi cycle as non-IE typological-breadth comparandum rather than as evidence for the formula itself, since the Chinese material is not an IE reflex; per brief 22-hou-yi.yaml on the peripheral-only contribution to Part II C5.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Multi-headed-monster motif (Lôtan / Leviathan, Ch. 7-8; Vṛtra, Ch. 14; Aži Dahāka, Ch. 13; Zmey Gorynych, Ch. 20; Yamata no Orochi, Ch. 23) — the Nine-headed Monster (*Jiuying*) of the Hou Yi cycle joins the wider cross-tradition family of multi-headed serpentine adversaries, although the early-Chinese material treats the killing in heroic-combat-pattern rather than cosmogonic-combat-pattern register.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Solar-multiplicity motif — the ten-suns cosmology of the Hou Yi cycle is a distinctively early-Chinese mythological feature with no close parallels in the present book's other chapters; the solar-plurality-rectification structure of the cycle is one of the cycle's principal contributions to the book's cross-cultural breadth.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Cosmogony / order-restoration distinction — the Hou Yi cycle's order-restoring (rather than cosmogonic) combat-pattern is a useful comparandum for the methods sidebar (Ch. 33) and for Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) on the boundary between order-establishing and order-restoring combat-narrative types; the Chinese material exhibits both types within a single tradition (Gonggong cosmogonic; Hou Yi restoring) and is therefore a useful internal-contrast case.
```

```
      heroic-combat-pattern rather than cosmogonic-combat-pattern."
    problem: >
      Zhuyin (燭陰, also written Zhulong 燭龍, the "Torch Dragon" or "Candlelight Dragon") is
      a distinct entity in the Shanhaijing (Classic of Mountains and Seas, ch. 17 Haineijing
      / Great Wilderness North). The Shanhaijing describes Zhuyin as a serpentine cosmic deity
      with a human face and red serpentine body whose opening and closing eyes create day and
      night and whose breathing produces the alternation of seasons — a figure of cosmic-clock
      significance, not a beast Hou Yi kills. Zhuyin is NOT listed in the Huainanzi
      (Benyingjun, ch. 8) as one of the beasts Hou Yi defeats. The Huainanzi's beast-catalogue
      for Hou Yi names: Zaochi (凿齿, Chiseltooth), Jiuying (九婴, Nine-headed figure), Dafeng
      (大风, Great Wind bird), Fengxi (封豨, Great Boar), and Xiushe (修蛇, Long Serpent at
      Dongting Lake). The 'serpent' in Hou Yi's repertoire is Xiushe (the Long Serpent),
      not Zhuyin.
      The document's framing "Zhuyin (sometimes alternatively named Xiushe or Bashe)" inverts
      the relationship: Xiushe and Bashe are the attested Hou Yi adversaries; Zhuyin is a
      cosmic entity in the Shanhaijing unconnected to Hou Yi's beast-slaying. This error
      appears to originate in the brief itself (brief 22-hou-yi.yaml variants.summary and
      summary both name "Zhuyin serpent" as one of the three representative beasts), so the
      claims document faithfully transmits the brief's error. The error propagates to all
      three beast-named claims, both VARIANT markers, and the COMPARATIVE-HOOK at line 160.
    evidence: >
      Huainanzi ch. 8 (Benyingjun, also transliterated as Benjing Xun) beast-list per trained
      knowledge: (1) Zaochi at Chouhua, (2) Jiuying at Xiongshui River, (3) Dafeng bird at
      Qingqiu Lake, (4) Fengxi boar at Sanglin, (5) Xiushe at Dongting Lake. No Zhuyin.
      Shanhaijing Zhuyin / Zhulong entry per trained knowledge: a cosmic serpentine deity whose
      blinking controls day and night; listed in the Great Wilderness North section. Not connected
      to the Hou Yi beast-catalogue. Birrell 1993 ch. 5 ("The Solar Myth") covers both the Hou
      Yi material and the Shanhaijing cosmological entities but does not identify Zhuyin as a
      Hou Yi adversary. Lewis 2006 on the Yao-era beast-catalogue: the Huainanzi list (including
      Xiushe as the serpent) is the standard scholarly synthesis; Zhuyin is not in the Lewis 2006
      synthesis of Hou Yi beasts per trained knowledge of the book.
    recommended_fix: >
      Replace "the Zhuyin serpent (sometimes alternatively named Xiushe or Bashe)" with
      "the Long Serpent (Chinese Xiushe, sometimes also referred to as Bashe in different
      strata of the textual tradition)" throughout the claims document and in the VARIANT
      markers. The brief (22-hou-yi.yaml) should be corrected at the same time — the
      brief's variants.summary and summary fields should read "Xiushe (Long Serpent)" not
      "Zhuyin serpent." The change is a name-substitution in three body claims and two
      VARIANT markers. The COMPARATIVE-HOOK at line 160 should similarly be updated to
      read "Xiushe / Long Serpent" rather than implicitly sustaining the Zhuyin framing.
      At human review, verify against Birrell 1993 ch. 5 and Lewis 2006 (Hou Yi / Yao
      cluster section) that Xiushe (and not Zhuyin) is the serpentine adversary in the
      Hou Yi beast-slaying tradition.

  - id: FC-02
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Source overview" — the claim at line 34 that Lewis 2006 "treats the Hou Yi / Yao
      cluster as one of the early-Chinese order-restoring narrative complexes alongside
      the Gonggong cosmogonic combat of the preceding chapter and supplies the present
      chapter's working synthesis across the Chuci, Huainanzi, and Shanhaijing witnesses."
      The implicit claim that Lewis 2006 provides a synthesis-level treatment of the Hou Yi
      beast-catalogue (named beasts Zaochi, Jiuying, and the serpent) is supported by 30
      EVIDENCE tags citing "lewis-2006-flood ; loc=Hou Yi / Yao cluster" with no chapter
      numbers.
    claim_text: >
      "The chapter's principal scholarly anchor is Mark Edward Lewis's The Flood Myths
      of Early China, published by SUNY Press in 2006, which treats the Hou Yi / Yao cluster
      as one of the early-Chinese order-restoring narrative complexes alongside the Gonggong
      cosmogonic combat of the preceding chapter and supplies the present chapter's working
      synthesis across the Chuci, Huainanzi, and Shanhaijing witnesses."
    problem: >
      Lewis 2006 "The Flood Myths of Early China" is a book primarily focused on flood myths
      (the Gun-Yu tradition and the hydraulic cosmological ordering). The Hou Yi solar and
      beast-slaying material is treated in Lewis 2006 as Yao-era contextual narrative — it
      appears as part of the broader Yao-era ordering-narrative discussion rather than as
      Lewis 2006's primary subject matter. The sources.yaml entry for lewis-2006-flood notes
      "Anchor for Gonggong and the Yu-sidebar" — not for Hou Yi — reflecting the book's actual
      primary focus. The brief overrides this for Ch22 (specifying Lewis 2006 with "Hou Yi /
      Yao cluster" as the loc), so the claims document is brief-authorised; but the brief itself
      does not supply specific chapter numbers (contrast: the Gonggong brief specifies "chs.
      2-3" with precision).
      The 30 EVIDENCE tags citing Lewis 2006 at the vague "Hou Yi / Yao cluster" level are
      consistent with the brief's citation level but create a risk that specific beast-name
      claims — particularly the individual beast names (Zaochi, Jiuying, and the serpent),
      the "four directional zones" Shanhaijing geographical placement, and the "variation in
      killing-method" claim — may be overstating Lewis 2006's actual treatment depth for the
      Hou Yi beast-catalogue vs. the flood material.
    evidence: >
      sources.yaml lewis-2006-flood note: "Anchor for Gonggong and the Yu-sidebar." No mention
      of Hou Yi. Brief 22-hou-yi.yaml specifies Lewis 2006 at "Hou Yi / Yao cluster" level with
      no chapter numbers. The Gonggong brief (21-gonggong.yaml) specifies Lewis 2006 at "chs.
      2-3" with high specificity. The asymmetry in brief-level citation discipline between Ch21
      and Ch22 means Ch22's claims cannot be verified to specific Lewis 2006 pages/chapters at
      this audit stage.
    recommended_fix: >
      At human review, open Lewis 2006 and identify which chapter(s) treat the Hou Yi / Yao
      cluster material. Update the brief (22-hou-yi.yaml) source loc to include specific chapter
      numbers (e.g., "ch. 1" or "chs. 1-2" if Lewis discusses Hou Yi in the opening Yao-era
      section), matching the precision of the Gonggong brief. Once chapter numbers are pinned,
      verify that the beast-name specifics (Zaochi, Jiuying, Xiushe — corrected from Zhuyin per
      FC-01) and the Shanhaijing four-directional-zones geographical claim are actually in Lewis
      2006 at the cited section rather than being inferred from the Huainanzi/Shanhaijing primary
      texts alone without Lewis's synthesis. This is a MEDIUM (not HIGH) finding because Lewis
      2006 is confirmed as treating Yao-era material broadly; the risk is depth-of-coverage, not
      wrong-source.

  - id: FC-03
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: unverified
    location: >
      COMPARATIVE-HOOK comment at line 162, citing "the methods sidebar (Ch. 33)" for the
      Cosmogony / order-restoration distinction comparandum. Also referenced in body claim
      at lines 144-145.
    claim_text: >
      "// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Cosmogony / order-restoration distinction — the Hou Yi cycle's
      order-restoring (rather than cosmogonic) combat-pattern is a useful comparandum for

    problem: >
      The Methods sidebar is described in scope.md as placed "between Part I and Part II" with

      total count higher but none is numbered 33. The chapter number "Ch. 33" for the Methods
      sidebar cannot be confirmed from the catalog or scope.md.
    evidence: >

      method, appropriation protocol, area-specialist citation policy." No chapter number
      assigned. Catalog briefs directory: 00 through 29 plus Part II comparative chapters (C1-C8)
      whose ordinal numbering has not been finalized. No brief file exists for a chapter 33.
    recommended_fix: >
      In both the COMPARATIVE-HOOK and any body claim referencing the Methods sidebar by
      chapter number, replace "Ch. 33" with "[Methods sidebar, Part I-II transition, chapter
      number TBC at catalog-lock]" or similar. The sidebar itself is a real and well-defined
      structural element of the book; only the chapter number is unconfirmed. This fix is
      confined to the comment lines and will not affect the chapter draft's body prose, which
      should refer to the sidebar descriptively rather than by number until catalog-lock.

  - id: FC-04
    severity: LOW
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Aftermath" — the claim at line 138 comparing the Hou Yi beast-slaying cycle to
      "the Greek Herakles labours sequence (chapter 12 of the present volume on Greek
      dragon-combats after Zeus-Typhon, which treats Herakles among the wider family of
      Greek dragon-slayers)."
    claim_text: >
      "The cycle's combat-pattern is structurally closer to the Greek Herakles labours sequence
      (chapter 12 of the present volume on Greek dragon-combats after Zeus-Typhon, which treats
      Herakles among the wider family of Greek dragon-slayers) than to the cosmogonic-combat
      patterns of the present book's CORE chapters: in both the Hou Yi cycle and the Herakles
      labours, a hero-figure works through a sequence of monstrous adversaries whose individual
      killings together accomplish the cycle's order-making work, with no single adversary
      functioning as the paramount cosmogonic opponent."
    problem: >
      scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs" explicitly excludes "Hero-initiation monster fights
      (Fionn vs. Aillen, most of Herakles's non-Hydra labours outside the serpent-combat corpus)
      — order-making for the hero, not for the cosmos." The body claim compares the Hou Yi
      cycle to "the Herakles labours sequence" without the scoping qualifier that limits the
      comparison to the serpent-combat sub-set within Ch12 (Hydra, Ladon, Geryon). As written,
      the comparison could be read as encompassing the full twelve labours, most of which are
      scope-excluded. The COMPARATIVE-HOOK at line 156 correctly specifies "a hero-figure works
      through a series of monstrous opponents" — which implicitly covers the serpent-subset —
      but the body claim's reference to "the Herakles labours sequence" broadly creates an
      internal tension with the scope exclusion that the chapter draft should resolve.
    evidence: >
      scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs" item 2: "Hero-initiation monster fights (Fionn vs.
      Aillen, most of Herakles's non-Hydra labours outside the serpent-combat corpus) — order-
      making for the hero, not for the cosmos." Ch12 brief (12-greek-dragons-after-typhon.yaml)
      treats "Herakles/Hydra-Ladon-Geryon" as the serpent-subset within the family chapter.
      The comparison should be scoped to this subset, not "the labours sequence" broadly.
    recommended_fix: >
      In the body claim (line 138), replace "the Greek Herakles labours sequence (chapter 12
      of the present volume on Greek dragon-combats after Zeus-Typhon, which treats Herakles
      among the wider family of Greek dragon-slayers)" with something like: "the Greek serpent-
      combat cluster associated with Herakles (Hydra, Ladon, Geryon — treated in chapter 12 of
      the present volume on Greek dragon-combats after Zeus-Typhon)." This scoping aligns the
      comparison with the scope exclusion and is accurate to Ch12's actual content. This is LOW
      rather than MEDIUM because the intended comparandum is clearly the serpent-combat subset
      and the error is imprecision rather than a substantive factual claim.

  - claim_number: 1

      The chapter treats the Chinese hero-narrative of Hou Yi as a related-tier (short full)
      chapter rounding out the East Asian cluster by exhibiting a different Chinese combat-
      pattern from the cosmogonic Gonggong combat of the preceding chapter.
    source_id: lewis-2006-flood
    source_loc: Hou Yi / Yao cluster
    verbatim_quote: >

      than cosmogonic Chinese narrative, placed after Gonggong in the East Asian cluster,
      is consistent with Lewis 2006's treatment of Yao-era ordering narratives as a distinct
      complex from the cosmogonic Gonggong material. Confirmed via trained knowledge of the
      book's organisation.

  - claim_number: 2
    claim: >
      The Hou Yi material is a composite reconstructed from several Warring States and
      Han-period sources: Chuci 'Tian wen', the Huainanzi, and the Shanhaijing, each preserving
      different elements of the cycle.
    source_id: lewis-2006-flood
    source_loc: Hou Yi / Yao cluster
    verbatim_quote: >
      [printed-only] — The composite-editorial character of the Hou Yi material across three

      scholarship and is confirmed in Lewis 2006 and Birrell 1993 per trained knowledge. The
      absence of a single complete primary-text narrative is one of the chapter's well-
      established textual features.

  - claim_number: 3
    claim: >
      The earliest principal textual witness is the Chuci ('Songs of Chu') poem Tian wen
      ('Heavenly Questions'), conventionally dated to approximately the third century BCE,

      the episode.
    source_id: birrell-1993

      per trained knowledge.

summary:
  verdict: MINOR-REVISE
  verdict_reason: >
    One HIGH finding (FC-01) and two MEDIUM findings (FC-02, FC-03), one LOW finding (FC-04).
    The HIGH finding (FC-01) is a content error — "Zhuyin serpent" is presented as one of
    Hou Yi's three named adversaries, but Zhuyin (Zhulong, the Torch Dragon) is a Shanhaijing
    cosmic entity unrelated to the Hou Yi beast-killing tradition; the attested Hou Yi serpent-
    adversary is Xiushe (the Long Serpent). This error originates in the brief itself and
    propagates throughout the claims document. The fix is a name-substitution throughout the
    document and the brief. The MEDIUM findings are: (FC-02) Lewis 2006 lacks chapter-number
    specificity in the brief, creating unverifiable coverage depth for the beast-catalogue
    detail claims; and (FC-03) the Methods sidebar is cited as "Ch. 33" but no such chapter
    number is confirmed in the catalog or scope.md. The LOW finding is imprecision in the
    Herakles comparison that could create scope-friction in the chapter draft. The verdict is
    MINOR-REVISE rather than MAJOR-REVISE because there is only one HIGH finding, no
    contamination, no fabrication, no silent bridges, and the framing and marker discipline
    are strong throughout.

  order_restoring_framing_assessment: PASS
  order_restoring_framing_notes: >
    The order-restoring framing is this chapter's most important success. The claims
    consistently distinguish the Hou Yi cycle's "order-under-threat" character from the
    cosmogonic-combat pattern of the CORE chapters. The pre-existing cosmos and pre-existing
    Yao sovereignty are threaded through every relevant claim. The chapter correctly identifies
    itself as a related-tier case and articulates why: the suns and beasts are not a single
    cosmogonic adversary, the combat is heroic-restorative rather than world-establishing, and
    the cycle's four-subtype non-fit (not cosmogonic, not sovereignty-establishing, not
    cyclical, not eschatological) is explicitly stated. The order-restoring credential is
    robustly established; the chapter clearly earns its slot in the book.

  invariant_check: >
    CONFIRMED. 42 footnotes / 42 EVIDENCE tokens. 1:1 invariant holds exactly.

  findings_summary:
    total: 4
    HIGH: 1      # FC-01 (Zhuyin identity wrong — should be Xiushe)
    MEDIUM: 2    # FC-02 (Lewis 2006 lacks chapter-number specificity for Hou Yi beast-catalogue)
                 # FC-03 (Methods sidebar cited as "Ch. 33" — not confirmed in catalog)
    LOW: 1       # FC-04 (Herakles "labours sequence" comparison too broad; scope-friction risk)
    claims_with_issues: 5   # 3 beast-named claims + COMPARATIVE-HOOK at line 160 (FC-01);
                            # Methods sidebar chapter number (FC-03)
    claims_without_issues: 37

  most_consequential_finding: >
    FC-01: The third named beast is presented as "the Zhuyin serpent (sometimes alternatively
    named Xiushe or Bashe)" — but Zhuyin (燭陰/燭龍, the Torch Dragon / Candlelight Dragon)
    is a Shanhaijing cosmic deity whose eyes control day and night; it is NOT an adversary
    that Hou Yi kills in either the Huainanzi or the standard Shanhaijing reading. The Hou Yi
    serpent-adversary is Xiushe (修蛇, the Long Serpent), killed at Dongting Lake per the
    Huainanzi ch. 8 beast-catalogue. The error originates in the brief and propagates to three
    body claims, two VARIANT markers, and a COMPARATIVE-HOOK. The fix is a name-substitution
    throughout both the claims document and the brief; it is straightforward but must be applied
    consistently.
```

```
chapter_number: 21
slug: "gonggong"
title: "Gonggong and the Broken Pillar"
chapter_anchor: "ch-21-gonggong"

sources:
  primary:
    - id: lewis-2006-flood
      loc: "The Flood Myths of Early China, chs. 2–3 (Gonggong, Nüwa, and the broken-pillar cosmogony); and chapters on Yu the Great used for the sidebar"
      translator_edition: "Lewis 2006"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780791466636"
      url_or_file: ~
    - id: birrell-1993
      loc: "Chinese Mythology: An Introduction, chapters on cosmogony"
      translator_edition: "Birrell 1993"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780801849954"
      url_or_file: ~
  secondary:
    - { id: forsyth-1987, loc: "on non-Near-Eastern combat-myth" }
    - { id: scurlock-beal-2013, loc: "limited references to Chinese material" }

lacunae:
  - source: lewis-2006-flood
    location: "Huainanzi 天文訓; Lienü zhuan (2nd–1st c. BCE)"
    what_is_missing: "Narrative details vary significantly across the Han compilations; no primary Warring States source preserves a complete Gonggong narrative"
    in_tradition_fills_available: "partial — the Shanhaijing, Huainanzi, and later commentaries together give a partial composite"

variants:
  classification: co-equal
  prevalent_version:
    source: lewis-2006-flood
    why_prevalent: "Lewis 2006 is the standard English-language synthesis of the Gonggong cycle. The Huainanzi is the fullest single account but is itself a 2nd c. BCE compilation of earlier material."
  alternates:
    - source: birrell-1993
      difference: "Huainanzi version: Gonggong fights Zhuanxu (or in some versions Zhurong) and loses; in rage, he strikes Mount Buzhou, breaking the sky-pillar and tilting the cosmos. Nüwa repairs with five-colored stones and the legs of a giant turtle"
    - source: lewis-2006-flood
      difference: "Lienü zhuan and other Han sources: Gonggong's combat is followed by the flood, which Yu the Great tames by opening channels — a sequential cosmogonic drama rather than two separate stories"
    - source: birrell-1993
      difference: "Shanhaijing and commentaries: different opponents for Gonggong (Zhuanxu, Zhurong, and others); fragmented into multiple mini-traditions"

entities_glossary:
  - name: "Gonggong (共工)"
    role: deity
    first_mention_gloss_candidate: "water-god, often red-haired and serpent-bodied; the defeated combatant who strikes the sky-pillar"
  - name: "Zhuanxu (顓頊)"
    role: deity
    first_mention_gloss_candidate: "the sky-emperor who defeats Gonggong in the most widely attested version"
  - name: "Nüwa (女媧)"
    role: deity
    first_mention_gloss_candidate: "serpent-tailed creator-goddess; repairs the broken sky with five-coloured stones"
  - name: "Mount Buzhou (不周山)"
    role: place
    first_mention_gloss_candidate: "'mountain-that-is-not-whole'; the pillar of heaven, broken by Gonggong's head-blow"
  - name: "Yu the Great (大禹)"
    role: hero
    first_mention_gloss_candidate: "post-combat flood-controller; his hydraulic ordering is the sidebar contrast in this chapter"
  - name: "five-coloured stones (五色石)"
    role: artifact
    first_mention_gloss_candidate: "the stones Nüwa melts to patch the sky"

cultural_relevance:
  centrality: central
  centrality_evidence:
    - kind: attestation-volume
      detail: "The Gonggong narrative is preserved across multiple Han-era compilations (Huainanzi, Lienü zhuan, commentaries on the Shanhaijing, Liezi) — the single most widely attested early-imperial Chinese cosmogonic combat"
      source: "lewis-2006-flood; birrell-1993"
    - kind: scholarly-attention
      detail: "Lewis 2006 treats the Gonggong narrative as the standard Chinese cosmogonic-combat comparandum in the comparative flood / cosmogony literature"
      source: "lewis-2006-flood"
    - kind: ritual-context
      detail: "Less directly liturgical than Near Eastern cases, but the cosmogony is cited in Han ritual and political texts to explain the asymmetric tilt of the cosmos (why rivers flow east)"
      source: "lewis-2006-flood"
  function:
    - role: cosmological
      basis: "The combat-plus-repair explains the present asymmetry of the cosmos: heaven tilts northwest, earth tilts southeast, and rivers flow east to the sea"
      source: "lewis-2006-flood; birrell-1993"
    - role: royal-legitimation
      basis: "Sky-emperors (Zhuanxu, Zhurong) defeat Gonggong as legitimate rulers; the combat is part of the succession of pre-human emperors"
      source: "lewis-2006-flood"
  cross_cultural_parallels:
    - other_culture: "Mesopotamia (Marduk)"
      parallel: "Both are combat-cosmogonies in the strong sense: the present features of the cosmos (asymmetry, rivers, geography) emerge from the combat's aftermath. Part II C1 pairs them"
      source: "lewis-2006-flood; lambert-2013"
    - other_culture: "Mesoamerica (Huitzilopochtli)"
      parallel: "Both Gonggong and Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec are combat-cosmogonies with explicit geographical aetiology (Mount Buzhou; Templo Mayor = Coatepec)"
      source: "lewis-2006-flood; matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor"
    - other_culture: "Sumer (Ninurta)"
      parallel: "Gonggong's water-releasing-through-broken-earth parallels Ninurta's post-combat reordering of the Tigris — both are storm-or-water-gods shaping hydrography through combat"
      source: "vandijk-1983-lugale; lewis-2006-flood"

comparative_hooks:
  - other_culture: "Mesopotamia / Mesoamerica"
    parallel: "Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) pairs Gonggong with Marduk, Ninurta/Asag, and Huitzilopochtli as the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases across non-cognate traditions"
    source: "lewis-2006-flood; lambert-2013; matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor"
  - other_culture: "China (Yu)"
    parallel: "The Yu sidebar within this chapter contrasts the combat-ordering of Gonggong with the hydraulic-ordering of Yu: the same tradition producing two different templates for cosmic order"
    source: "lewis-2006-flood"

triangulation_databases:
  - kotenbunko

target_length_words: 7000

special_instructions: |
  The chapter has an embedded Yu-the-Great sidebar (per scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 9).
  The sidebar's job is to show that within the same Chinese tradition, two different templates
  for cosmic ordering coexist — combat (Gonggong) and channel-opening (Yu). Do not expand the
  sidebar into a chapter of its own; keep it as a 800–1200-word section within Chapter 21.
  Present the Huainanzi and Lienü zhuan variants side by side; neither is scholarly-privileged.
  Include the post-combat geography (tilted cosmos, rivers flowing east) as the cosmogonic
  outcome — this is the chapter's cosmogonic credential for Part II C1. Area-specialist
  citation policy applies: cite Lewis and Birrell by name in the first factual pass.
```

```
chapter_number: 22
slug: "hou-yi"
title: "Hou Yi and the Suns"
chapter_anchor: "ch-22-hou-yi"

chapter_type: "related (short full)"

sources:
  primary:
    - id: lewis-2006-flood
      loc: "The Flood Myths of Early China (Hou Yi / Yao cluster)"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780791466636"
    - id: birrell-1993
      loc: "Chinese Mythology, ch. on solar myths"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780801849954"

variants:
  classification: co-equal
  summary: |
    The Hou Yi material is preserved across Chuci 'Tian wen' (c. 3rd c. BCE), the Huainanzi
    (2nd c. BCE), and the Shanhaijing, each with different emphases. No single source has
    the complete narrative. The nine-beasts subnarrative (Chiseltooth, Nine-headed Monster,
    Zhuyin serpent) varies across sources. Treat as composite-editorial with Lewis 2006
    as the scholarly synthesis.

summary: |
  When ten suns rise together and scorch the earth, the archer Hou Yi shoots down nine of
  them and also slays monstrous beasts (the Chiseltooth, the Nine-headed Monster, the
  Zhuyin serpent). Order is restored under the sage-king Yao.

cultural_relevance:
  centrality: significant (within Chinese mythic repertoire; central as narrative of the Yao era)
  function: |
    - cosmological (solar-plurality rectification, environmental restoration)
    - royal-legitimation (Yao's era needs order)
    - didactic (the archer-hero as exemplar)
  cross_cultural_parallels: |
    - The beast-slaying cluster parallels the Herakles labours (Chapter 12 family): hero
      works through a series of monstrous opponents.
    - Unlike the Gonggong combat (Chapter 21), Hou Yi is not cosmogonic — the cosmos exists
      and the hero restores order within it.

target_length_words: 3500

special_instructions: |
  Short full chapter. The chapter's role is to round out the East Asian cluster by showing
  a different Chinese combat-pattern from Gonggong: not a cosmogonic combat but a heroic
  sequence of order-restoring slayings. The nine-suns episode and the monster-beasts are
  distinguishable but co-occurring in the tradition — the chapter presents both and notes
  their relation. Do not pad. Feeds Part II C5 (IE formula) only peripherally — Hou Yi is
  not an IE reflex — but contributes to the book's cross-cultural breadth.
```
